The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Inferences in Two-Part Contradictory Disjunctions
Author demonstrates the triviality of syllogisms with contradictory disjunctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why a disjunctive syllogism with contradictory parts yields a trivial conclusion and is not useful.

Core Idea
When the two parts are contradictory, the conclusion is the same as the premise, making the syllogism useless.

The author examines disjunctions where the two parts are direct negations of each other, like 'every number is either even or not even.' In such cases, excepting either part or its negation leads to a tautology. This lesson explains why such syllogisms are not useful for gaining new knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify a disjunction with contradictory parts.
Look for a two-part disjunction where one part is the negation of the other, e.g., 'A or not A'.
The author gives the example: 'Every number is either even or not even.'
2
Apply the exception (istithna') of one part.
If you affirm one part (say 'it is not even'), what follows? According to the author, the conclusion is the opposite part ('it is even'). But note that this conclusion is logically equivalent to the negation of the excepted part.
3
Check if the conclusion provides new information.
Since the conclusion is logically the same as the excepted premise (just rephrased), it is trivial. The author states that such syllogisms are 'not useful' (ghayru muntafi' bih).
Only useful if the parts are not contradictory in this manner.

The Syllogism
intermediate
5 min
Inferences in Two-Part Non-Contradictory Disjunctions
Author gives rules for non-contradictory two-part disjunctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a valid disjunctive syllogism with two non-contradictory parts and identify the conditions for its validity.

Core Idea
In non-contradictory disjunctions, affirming one part negates the other, and negating one affirms the other, provided the disjunction is universal and real.

Now the author turns to disjunctions where the two parts are not direct negations, like 'Every number is either even or odd.' Here, the inference yields new information: affirming one part entails the negation of the other, and vice versa. But there are conditions: the disjunction must be universal and real (haqiqi), not accidental.

Guided Path
1
Verify that the disjunction has two non-contradictory parts.
Check that the two parts are not the negation of each other. The author gives the example 'Every number is either even or odd.'
2
Apply the exception (istithna') of one part.
If you affirm one part (e.g., 'it is even'), then the conclusion is the negation of the other part ('it is not odd'). If you negate one part ('it is not even'), the conclusion is affirmation of the other ('it is odd').
This is the rule for non-contradictory parts.
3
Check the conditions: universality, time specification, and reality.
The inference is valid only if the disjunction is universal (i.e., applies to all instances), the time of the disjunction is specified (to avoid temporal ambiguity), and the disjunction is real (haqiqi) rather than accidental (ittifaqi). A real disjunction means the parts are genuinely mutually exclusive and exhaustive.
If the disjunction is accidental, the inference may not hold.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الانفصال غير متناقضة هذا النوع من التناقض. وإن كانت لا على هذا النحو من التناقض، كقولنا : (كل) عدد فهو إما زوج وإما (فرد فاستثناء عين كل واحد منهما ينتج نقيض الآخر، لكن شرط أن تكون كلية أو أن يكون وقت الانفصال معيناً، وإلا فالحكم بعين أخذ الجزأين، قد يكون لا في وقت الحكم بالانفصال، وإذ ذاك فلا يلزم الإنتاج، وأن يكون حقيقياً لا اتفاقياً فإنك إذا قلت لكنه زوج لزمه أنه ليس بفرد) وإن قلت إنه فرد، لزمه أنه ليس بزوج) واستثناء نقيض أيهما كان ينتج عين الآخر، فإنك إذا قلت إنه ليس بزوج) لزمه إنه (فرد) وإن قلت إنه ليس بفرد) لزمه أنه (زوج). وإن كانت أجزاء الانفصال أكثر من اثنين فإما أن تكون متناهية أو غير متناهية فإن كانت متناهية، كقولنا : كل متحرك إما جماد أو نبات أو حيوان، فإما أن تستثني العين أو النقيض من كل واحد منها، فإن استثنيت العين بأن قلت لكنه (حيوان) فقد يتشكك عليه بأنه إما أن يكون المطلوب أنه ليس بجماد ولا نبات فهي نتائج متعددة، والنتائج المتعددة معاً بالذات لا تكون لازمة عن قياس واحد، وإن جعل المطلوب أنه ليس إما جماد وإما (نبات كان الإنتاج كاذباً، إذ ليس حقاً أن المتحرك ليس إما جماد وإما نبات والحق أنه إن جعلت النتائج متكثرة على النحو المقول، فإنه وإن لم يمتنع من مجرد ما ذكروه، ولكنه إنما يتم بتقدير مقدمات في الذهن تقترن بقولنا: إنه (حيوان) لو لم يحصل إقرانها في الذهن به لَما استبان صدق النتائج وهي (ولا شيء من الحيوان (نبات) و (لا شيء من الحيوان (جماد فلولا تقدير (لا) شيء من الحيوان (جماد لما استبان صدق (لا شيء من المتحرك (جماد ولولا تقدير ولا شيء من الحيوان نبات لما استبان صدق إنه لا شيء من المتحرك نبات لكنه لما حذفت هذه المقدمات اعتماداً على استقرارها ذهناً، قد يظن أن النتائج المتكثرة لازمة عن قياس متحد. وإن جعلت النتيجة متحدة سالبة للانفصال بين الجماد والنبات بالنسبة إلى المتحرك، فهو أيضاً حق وليس بكذب، إذ قيل أن المتحرك حيوان فلو لم يصدق أنه ليس إما جماد وإما (نبات إلا كان بعض ما هو حيوان إما جماد وإما نبات وهو ظاهر الإحالة. وأما استثناء النقيض من كل واحد من الأجزاء فإنه منتج إنتاجاً ظاهراً موجبة
As for the conditional being disjunctive, it is either affirmative or negative. If affirmative, it is either exclusive (man'iat al-jam' wa al-khuluw), or inclusive of concurrence (man'iat al-khuluw duna al-jam'), or exclusive of concurrence (man'iat al-jam' duna al-khuluw). It either has two parts or more. If it has two parts, they are either contradictory in the sense of negation and affirmation or not. If they are contradictory in the sense of negation and affirmation, like our saying: 'Every number is either even or not even,' then the exception of the very one of them or its negation yields its very opposite. For if you except the very thing that is not even, it entails the negation of the other part, which is that it is 'even,' which is the very thing excepted. And if you say it is 'even,' it entails that it is not 'not even,' and the negation of the negation of evenness is an affirmation of evenness, which is the very thing excepted. And if it were other than that, I do not know it. This is if you except. And if you except the negation from each of them to produce the very other, it does not occur except with the very other, for it is its negation. Thus the sought is the very thing that occurs as excepted in the syllogism, and it has no meaning. So it is necessary that the syllogism on these forms is not useful, unless the parts of the disjunction are not contradictory in this type of contradiction. And if they are not in this manner of contradiction, like our saying: 'Every number is either even or odd,' then the exception of the very each of them produces the negation of the other, but on condition that it is universal or that the time of disjunction is specific. Otherwise, the judgment of the very taking of the two parts may not be at the time of the judgment of disjunction, and then production does not follow, and that it be real (haqiqi) not accidental (ittifaqi). For if you say 'but it is even,' it entails that it is not odd. And if you say it is odd, it entails that it is not even. And the exception of the negation of either of them produces the very other. For if you say it is not even, it entails that it is odd. And if you say it is not odd, it entails that it is even. And if the parts of the disjunction are more than two, they are either finite or infinite. If they are finite, like our saying: 'Every moving thing is either inanimate, or plant, or animal,' then you either except the very or the negation from each of them. If you except the very by saying 'but it is animal,' then it may be doubted that the sought is either that it is not inanimate and not plant, which are multiple results, and multiple results together essentially are not necessary from a single syllogism. And if the sought is made a single negative of the disjunction between inanimate and plant with respect to the moving thing, then the production is false, for it is not true that the moving thing is not either inanimate or plant. And the truth is that if the results are made multiple in the said manner, then even if it is not impossible from mere what they mentioned, it is only completed by assuming premises in the mind that are conjoined with our saying 'it is animal.' If their conjunction in the mind with it did not occur, the truth of the results would not be apparent, and they are: 'Nothing of the animal is plant' and 'Nothing of the animal is inanimate.' For without assuming 'Nothing of the animal is inanimate,' the truth of 'Nothing of the moving thing is inanimate' would not be apparent, and without assuming 'Nothing of the animal is plant,' the truth that 'Nothing of the moving thing is plant' would not be apparent. But since these premises are omitted, relying on their stability in the mind, it might be thought that multiple results are necessary from a single syllogism. And if the result is made a single negative of the disjunction between inanimate and plant with respect to the moving thing, it is also true and not false, for if it is said that the moving thing is animal, then if it were not true that it is not either inanimate or plant, then some of what is animal would be either inanimate or plant, which is obviously impossible. As for the exception of the negation from each of the parts, it produces an affirmative production evidently.
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of disjunctive conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i munfasil). The author begins by classifying disjunctive conditionals as either affirmative or negative. Affirmative ones are further divided into three types: exclusive that prevents both concurrence and void (man'iat al-jam' wa al-khuluw, i.e., exclusive disjunction), inclusive that prevents void without preventing concurrence (man'iat al-khuluw duna al-jam', i.e., inclusive disjunction), and exclusive that prevents concurrence without preventing void (man'iat al-jam' duna al-khuluw, i.e., another type). Then the author focuses on disjunctions with two parts, which can be contradictory (i.e., one is the negation of the other) or non-contradictory. For contradictory parts, exemplified by 'Every number is either even or not even', the rule is that affirming (excepting the very) or negating (excepting the negation) one part directly yields the opposite, leading to a trivial conclusion that is the same as the excepted term. The author states that such syllogisms are not useful because the conclusion is not new. For non-contradictory parts, e.g., 'Every number is either even or odd', the rule is: affirming one part produces the negation of the other, and negating one produces the affirmation of the other, provided the disjunction is universal and the time is specified, and that it is a real (haqiqi) disjunction (not accidental). The author then discusses disjunctions with more than two parts, using the example 'Every moving thing is either inanimate, plant, or animal'. He explains the issues when one excepts the very part (e.g., 'but it is animal'): the conclusion appears to be multiple (e.g., it is not inanimate and not plant), but a single syllogism cannot yield multiple conclusions directly. The truth is that the multiple conclusions depend on unstated premises (like 'Nothing of the animal is plant') that are assumed in the mind. Alternatively, if the conclusion is taken as a single negation of the whole disjunction (i.e., the moving thing is not either inanimate or plant), that is actually true but requires the universal premise 'All animals are not inanimate or plant' which is universally true. He concludes that excepting the negation from each part produces an affirmative conclusion obviously.

Key Terms
مانعة الجمع والخلو
حقيقي
اتفاقي

Checkpoint

A) It is odd
B) It is not odd
C) It is not even
D) No entailment

Key Takeaway
Non-contradictory disjunctions allow valid inferences of affirmation-negation pairs, provided the disjunction is universal, time-bound, and real.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Syllogism
advanced
5 min
Inferences in Multi-Part Disjunctions
Author addresses the problem of multiple conclusions in multi-part disjunctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a single disjunctive syllogism with more than two parts cannot yield multiple conclusions directly, and identify the hidden premises needed.

Core Idea
A single disjunctive syllogism with more than two parts cannot produce multiple conclusions without relying on hidden universal premises.

When the disjunction has more than two parts, like 'every moving thing is either inanimate, plant, or animal,' the inference becomes complex. The author shows that if you except one part (say 'it is animal'), the apparent multiple conclusions (that it is not inanimate and not plant) actually depend on additional premises like 'nothing of the animal is inanimate.' This lesson explores that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify a multi-part disjunction with more than two parts.
The author gives an example with three parts: 'Every moving thing is either inanimate, plant, or animal.'
2
Apply the exception (istithna') of one part.
If you except the part 'animal' (i.e., affirm it is animal), what conclusions seem to follow? It appears that it is not inanimate and not plant.
These are two separate conclusions.
3
Uncover the hidden premises.
The author argues that the conclusion 'it is not inanimate' depends on the premise 'nothing of the animal is inanimate,' and similarly for plant. These premises are assumed in the mind but are not part of the single syllogism.
Without explicitly stating these, the syllogism is incomplete.
4
Consider alternative formulations.
If the conclusion is taken as a single negative of the disjunction between inanimate and plant (i.e., 'the moving thing is not either inanimate or plant'), then it is valid, but that conclusion itself relies on the same hidden premises.
Alternatively, excepting the negation of each part yields an affirmative conclusion directly.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of disjunctive conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i munfasil). The author begins by classifying disjunctive conditionals as either affirmative or negative. Affirmative ones are further divided into three types: exclusive that prevents both concurrence and void (man'iat al-jam' wa al-khuluw, i.e., exclusive disjunction), inclusive that prevents void without preventing concurrence (man'iat al-khuluw duna al-jam', i.e., inclusive disjunction), and exclusive that prevents concurrence without preventing void (man'iat al-jam' duna al-khuluw, i.e., another type). Then the author focuses on disjunctions with two parts, which can be contradictory (i.e., one is the negation of the other) or non-contradictory. For contradictory parts, exemplified by 'Every number is either even or not even', the rule is that affirming (excepting the very) or negating (excepting the negation) one part directly yields the opposite, leading to a trivial conclusion that is the same as the excepted term. The author states that such syllogisms are not useful because the conclusion is not new. For non-contradictory parts, e.g., 'Every number is either even or odd', the rule is: affirming one part produces the negation of the other, and negating one produces the affirmation of the other, provided the disjunction is universal and the time is specified, and that it is a real (haqiqi) disjunction (not accidental). He explains the issues when one excepts the very part (e.g., 'but it is animal'): the conclusion appears to be multiple (e.g., it is not inanimate and not plant), but a single syllogism cannot yield multiple conclusions directly. He concludes that excepting the negation from each part produces an affirmative conclusion obviously.

The Syllogism
beginner

General Rule for Productive Exclusive Disjunction
Author states conditions for productive exclusive disjunction with example of even/odd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a disjunctive syllogism uses an exclusive disjunction and determine the valid conclusion.

Core Idea
Only exclusive (real) disjunction yields a valid syllogism; affirming one denies the other, and vice versa.

In this passage, the author lays down the fundamental rule for productive disjunctive syllogisms: the disjunction must be 'real' (exclusive), not accidental. Using the clear example of even and odd numbers, he shows how affirming one alternative necessarily denies the other, and denying one affirms the other. Let's walk through the logical structure.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction type
The passage specifies that the disjunction must be 'real' (haqiqi), meaning the two alternatives cannot be simultaneously true or false (exclusive or XOR). Determine if the disjunction in a given argument is exclusive or merely accidental (ittifaqi).
Accidental disjunctions do not produce valid syllogisms.
2
Apply the rule of affirmation
If one disjunct is affirmed (e.g., 'it is even'), then the conclusion is the negation of the other disjunct ('it is not odd'). This is one of the two valid inference patterns for exclusive disjunction.
3
Apply the rule of denial
If one disjunct is denied (e.g., 'it is not even'), then the conclusion is the affirmation of the other disjunct ('it is odd'). This is the second valid pattern.
The passage explicitly states both: 'exception of the negation of either produces the other'.
4
Verify productivity condition
The passage warns that productivity is not necessary if the disjunction is not real. Always check that the disjunction is exclusive before drawing any conclusion.
A real disjunction is one where the disjuncts are mutually exclusive and exhaustive.

وقت الحكم بالانفصال، وإذ ذاك فلا يلزم الإنتاج، وأن يكون حقيقياً لا اتفاقياً فإنك إذا قلت لكنه زوج لزمه أنه ليس بفرد) وإن قلت إنه فرد، لزمه أنه ليس بزوج) واستثناء نقيض أيهما كان ينتج عين الآخر، فإنك إذا قلت إنه ليس بزوج) لزمه إنه (فرد) وإن قلت إنه ليس بفرد) لزمه أنه (زوج).
The time of judgment of disjunction, and then productivity is not necessary, and that it be real not accidental. For if you say 'but it is even', it follows that it is not odd; and if you say 'it is odd', it follows that it is not even. And the exception of the negation of either produces the other: if you say 'it is not even', it follows that it is odd; and if you say 'it is not odd', it follows that it is even.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage defines the condition for a disjunctive syllogism to be productive: the disjunction must be real (haqiqi) or exclusive (mani'at al-jam' wal-khuluw), not accidental (ittifaqi). The example uses the pair 'even' and 'odd' (zawj and fard), which are truly exclusive. The passage shows two cases: (1) affirming one disjunct yields the denial of the other, and (2) denying one yields the affirmation of the other. This is the standard rule for exclusive disjunction.

Key Terms

استثناء

D) The disjunction becomes invalid

Key Takeaway
In exclusive (real) disjunction, affirming one alternative entails denying the other, and denying one entails affirming the other.

Exclusive Disjunction with Three or More Finite Parts
Author explains production rules for exclusive disjunction with >2 finite parts, including objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct valid conclusions from an exclusive disjunction with more than two finite parts and identify the implicit premises required for multiple conclusions.

Core Idea
Affirming one part of a multi-part exclusive disjunction requires implicit premises to yield multiple negative conclusions.

Exclusive disjunctions with more than two parts are common in Islamic legal reasoning (e.g., classifications of contracts or acts of worship). This passage explains how to correctly draw conclusions from such disjunctions, addressing a common objection about multiple conclusions. You will learn to distinguish between valid single conclusions and multiple conclusions that depend on suppressed premises.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction and its parts
Find the complete exclusive disjunction. In the text, the example is 'Every moving thing is either mineral, plant, or animal.' Note that the disjunction is exclusive (it cannot be more than one).
Exclusive disjunction means exactly one of the options is true.
2
Distinguish the two exception cases
The author considers two operations: (a) affirming one part (e.g., 'it is an animal'), and (b) denying one part (e.g., 'it is not an animal'). For each case, determine what conclusion is claimed.
Affirming one part aims to negate all others; denying one part affirms the remaining disjunction.
3
Analyze the objection to affirming one part
The objector says that if you affirm 'it is an animal' and want to conclude 'it is not mineral' and 'it is not plant', that is multiple conclusions from one syllogism, which is impossible. The author responds that these conclusions rely on implicit premises ('no animal is a mineral', 'no animal is a plant').
The implicit premises are universal negatives that connect the affirmed part to the other disjuncts.
4
Identify the valid single conclusion
Instead of multiple conclusions, the author presents a single valid conclusion: 'the moving thing is not either mineral or plant'. This avoids multiple conclusions and is logically equivalent.
This single negative disjunction is a valid immediate inference from the affirmed part plus the exclusive nature.
5
Apply the rule for denying one part
If you deny one part (e.g., 'it is not an animal'), the valid conclusion is a positive disjunction of the remaining parts: 'it is either mineral or plant'. This is straightforward.
No implicit premises needed; the conclusion follows directly from the exclusive disjunction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كانت أجزاء الانفصال أكثر من اثنين فإما أن تكون متناهية أو غير متناهية فإن كانت متناهية، كقولنا : كل متحرك إما جماد أو نبات أو حيوان، فإما أن تستثني العين أو النقيض من كل واحد منها، فإن استثنيت العين بأن قلت لكنه (حيوان) فقد يتشكك عليه بأنه إما أن يكون المطلوب أنه ليس بجماد ولا نبات فهي نتائج متعددة، والنتائج المتعددة معاً بالذات لا تكون لازمة عن قياس واحد، وإن جعل المطلوب أنه ليس إما جماد وإما (نبات كان الإنتاج كاذباً، إذ ليس حقاً أن المتحرك ليس إما جماد وإما نبات والحق أنه إن جعلت النتائج متكثرة على النحو المقول، فإنه وإن لم يمتنع من مجرد ما ذكروه، ولكنه إنما يتم بتقدير مقدمات في الذهن تقترن بقولنا: إنه (حيوان) لو لم يحصل إقرانها في الذهن به لَما استبان صدق النتائج وهي (ولا شيء من الحيوان (نبات) و (لا شيء من الحيوان (جماد فلولا تقدير (لا) شيء من الحيوان (جماد لما استبان صدق (لا شيء من المتحرك (جماد ولولا تقدير ولا شيء من الحيوان نبات لما استبان صدق إنه لا شيء من المتحرك نبات لكنه لما حذفت هذه المقدمات اعتماداً على استقرارها ذهناً، قد يظن أن النتائج المتكثرة لازمة عن قياس متحد وإن جعلت النتيجة متحدة سالبة للانفصال بين الجماد والنبات بالنسبة إلى المتحرك، فهو أيضاً حق وليس بكذب، إذ قيل أن المتحرك حيوان فلو لم يصدق أنه ليس إما جماد وإما (نبات إلا كان بعض ما هو حيوان إما جماد وإما نبات وهو ظاهر الإحالة. وأما استثناء النقيض من كل واحد من الأجزاء فإنه منتج إنتاجاً ظاهراً موجبة من الباقي بالشرط المذكور، وذلك كما إذا قلت لكنه ليس بحيوان) فالصادق أنه إما جماد وإما نبات
And if the parts of the disjunction are more than two, then they are either finite or infinite. If finite, like our saying: 'Every moving thing is either mineral, plant, or animal', then either you exclude the positive or the negation from each one. If you exclude the positive by saying 'but it is an animal', it might be objected that the desired conclusion is either 'it is not mineral and not plant' which are multiple conclusions, and multiple conclusions together essentially do not follow from one syllogism; and if the desired conclusion is made 'it is not either mineral or plant', the production would be false, for it is not true that the moving thing is not either mineral or plant. The truth is that if the conclusions are made multiple as said, then although it is not impossible from what they mentioned alone, it is only completed by supposing premises in the mind that accompany our saying 'it is an animal'. If those premises were not coupled in the mind with it, the truth of the conclusions would not be evident, which are 'no animal is a plant' and 'no animal is a mineral'. For without the supposition of 'no animal is a mineral', the truth of 'no moving thing is a mineral' would not be evident, and without the supposition of 'no animal is a plant', the truth of 'no moving thing is a plant' would not be evident. But because these premises are omitted relying on their being established in the mind, it might be thought that multiple conclusions follow from a single syllogism. And if the conclusion is made singular, a negative of the disjunction between mineral and plant with respect to the moving thing, that is also true and not false, for it is said that the moving thing is an animal; if it were not true that it is not either mineral or plant, then some of what is animal would be either mineral or plant, and that is obviously impossible. As for the exception of the negation from each part, it produces a positive disjunction from the rest with the mentioned condition, as when you say 'but it is not an animal' then the truth is that it is either mineral or plant.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage examines exclusive disjunction with more than two finite parts. It uses the example: 'Every moving thing is either mineral, plant, or animal' (a three-part exclusive disjunction). Two cases are considered: (1) Affirming one part (e.g., 'it is an animal'). A skeptic might argue that from this we cannot directly conclude multiple negative statements ('it is not mineral', 'it is not plant') as a single syllogism; the author responds that these conclusions depend on implicit premises (e.g., 'no animal is a mineral') that are assumed in the mind. Alternatively, we can form a single negative disjunction: 'it is not either mineral or plant', which is valid. (2) Denying one part (e.g., 'it is not an animal') yields a positive disjunction of the remaining parts ('it is either mineral or plant'). The author clarifies the epistemological process, showing that multiple explicit conclusions require additional premises that are often suppressed.

Key Terms
نتائج متعددة (nata'ij muta'addida)
مقدمات ذهنية (muqaddimat dhihniyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The moving thing is not mineral
B) No animal is a mineral
C) Every mineral is a moving thing
D) The moving thing is either mineral or plant

Key Takeaway
Exclusive disjunctions with more than two parts require careful handling: affirming one part yields a single negative disjunction of the rest, while multiple negative conclusions demand additional implicit premises.

Infinite Disjuncts in Exclusive Disjunction
Author argues that disjunction with infinite parts is impossible in language and thought.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why an exclusive disjunction with infinite parts is impossible in language and thought, and assess the consequences for syllogistic reasoning.

Core Idea
Infinite disjuncts cannot be uttered or conceived, so they cannot be used in a syllogism.

In Islamic logic, exclusive disjunctions are a key tool for dividing possibilities. But what if the disjuncts are infinite in number? The author tackles this extreme case, showing that such a disjunction is not just impractical but fundamentally impossible. Understanding this boundary clarifies why logical reasoning always relies on finite, manageable distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction type
The passage discusses 'exclusive disjunction' in which the parts are mutually exclusive. Recognize that the author is concerned with the case where the parts are infinite, like all natural numbers.
Exclusive disjunction here means the division is exhaustive and the parts are mutually exclusive.
2
Understand the condition of infinite parts
Parts are infinite (أجزاء غير متناهية) meaning they have no limit (لا حد لها). For the author, this makes it impossible to articulate the entire disjunction or even any single part.
Infinite in this context means a set with no finite bound, e.g., all numbers.
3
Analyze the impossibility of expression and conception
The author argues that what has no limit cannot be uttered (النطق به متعذر) not as a whole (لا بجملته) nor even each individual part (بل بكل واحد من أجزائه). Similarly, it cannot be conceived mentally (تعقله).
This is a strong claim: infinite parts are not only impractical but impossible in language and thought.
4
Connect the disjunction to syllogism
Every syllogism requires two categorical statements (قولان جازمان). Since an infinite-part disjunction cannot be formed, it cannot serve as a premise. Therefore, any syllogistic attempt using such a disjunction is futile (الاشتغال باستثمار مثل هذا غير مفيد).
The term 'استثمار' here refers to deriving conclusions from the disjunction via modus tollendo ponens.
5
Assess the general lesson
Conclude that logical operations, especially syllogisms, require finite and comprehensible disjunctions. Infinite disjunctions are a boundary case that logic cannot handle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كانت الأجزاء غير متناهية كمراتب الأعداد مثلاً، فإنه لا حد لها، وما لا حدّ له فالنطق به متعذر لا بجملته بل بكل واحد من أجزائه، وكذا أيضاً تعقله فلا يكون منه قضية منفصلة غير متناهية الأجزاء لا لفظاً ولا عقلاً، وكل قياس فلا بُدّ فيه من قولين جازمین فالاشتغال باستثمار مثل هذا غير مفيد
And if the parts are infinite, like the ranks of numbers for example, then they have no limit, and what has no limit, uttering it is impossible——not its entirety but each one of its parts——and similarly conceiving it, so there cannot be a disjunctive proposition with infinite parts, neither verbally nor mentally. And every syllogism must have two categorical statements, so occupying oneself with the exploitation of such [a disjunction] is useless.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage addresses the case of exclusive disjunction with infinite parts, such as the numbers. The author argues that since the parts are infinite, it is impossible to articulate or even conceive of them all. Therefore, no disjunctive proposition with infinite parts can be formed, and consequently no syllogism can use such a disjunction because every syllogism requires two categorical propositions. Hence, it is futile to consider such disjunctions in logic.

Key Terms
أجزاء غير متناهية
قولان جازمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
A disjunctive proposition with infinite parts is impossible both verbally and mentally, making it useless for syllogistic reasoning.

Progressive Exclusion in Multi-Part Disjunctions
Author explains the practical benefit of multi-part exclusive disjunctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply progressive exclusion to resolve a multi-part exclusive disjunction and derive a single conclusion.

Core Idea
Step-by-step exclusion of disjuncts pinpoints the conclusion.

Multi-part exclusive disjunctions are common in Islamic reasoning, from fiqh rulings to theological debates. This passage highlights their practical value: by systematically denying disjuncts, you can narrow down to a single remaining option. The author uses a number example and references an earlier case of animal, plant, and mineral to show how the method works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the complete disjunction
List all disjuncts that are mutually exclusive. In the passage, the example is 'Every number is either two, three, or what comes after that'.
The disjunction must be exhaustive for the narrowing to work.
2
Apply exceptions sequentially
Deny one disjunct at a time based on additional premises. For instance, if you have proof that it is not two and not three, you can gradually eliminate them.
Order does not matter; but each denial must be justified.
3
Conclude the remaining disjunct
After excluding all but one disjunct, that remaining disjunct is the conclusion. In the example, if both 'two' and 'three' are denied, the conclusion is 'what comes after that' (i.e., a number greater than 3).
The conclusion is certain if the disjunction is exclusive and the denials are correct.
4
Verify exclusivity
Ensure the disjunction is strictly exclusive: only one disjunct can be true at a time. Otherwise, multiple conclusions could remain.
The author implies the examples are exclusive (e.g., a number cannot be both 2 and >3).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إن أكثر الفوائد المحصلة من جنس هذا القياس إنما هو التدرج بالاستثناء مما حصل منه إلى نتيجة واحدة وقسم واحد، كقولنا: كل عدد إما اثنان أو ثلاثة أو ما بعد ذلك كان حكمها في الاستثناء عند الاستنتاج حكم ما تقدم من مثال الحيوان والنبات والجماد.
Then the greatest benefit obtained from this kind of syllogism is only the gradual [progression] by exception from what is obtained from it to a single conclusion and a single division, like our saying: 'Every number is either two or three or what comes after that'. Their ruling in exception at the time of inference is like the ruling that preceded from the example of animal, plant, and mineral.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage highlights the practical utility of multi-part exclusive disjunctions: by progressively excluding parts (i.e., denying them), one can eventually narrow down to a single conclusion. The example given is numbers: 'Every number is either two, three, or beyond'. Using exceptions, one can determine the exact number. The author notes that the rules for such syllogisms are analogous to the earlier example of animal, plant, and mineral.

Key Terms
التدرج بالاستثناء

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) To simplify definitions

Key Takeaway
Multi-part exclusive disjunctions are practically valuable for stepwise narrowing down to a definite conclusion.

Rules of Disjunctive Syllogism: Exclusive of Union (Mani'at al-Jam')
Author explains rules for disjunction that prevents conjunction but allows both false.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct valid syllogisms for disjunctions exclusive of union, identifying when a conclusion follows and when it does not.

Core Idea
In exclusive-union disjunctions, only affirming a part yields anything; denying yields nothing.

We now turn to a specific type of disjunctive syllogism: the 'exclusive of union' (mani'at al-jam') but not exclusive of emptiness. This means the parts cannot be true together, but they can all be false. Understanding this is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic jurisprudence and theology where alternatives may be incompatible yet not exhaustive. In this lesson, we will examine the rules for two-part and multi-part cases, as explained by the author.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction type
Confirm that the disjunction is exclusive of union only. Look for the phrase 'مانعة الجمع دون الخلو' meaning parts cannot be true together but can all be false.
2
Apply the rule for two-part disjunctions
If there are two parts, check the exception: affirming one part yields the negation of the other; denying one part yields no conclusion.
Example: 'المتحرك إما جماد أو نبات' – if you affirm 'it is a plant', then 'it is not a mineral' follows; but denying 'it is not a mineral' does not give 'it is a plant'.
3
Apply the rule for multi-part disjunctions
If there are more than two distinct parts, affirming one part yields the negation of all others; denying one part yields no conclusion.
Example: 'كل جسم بسيط فهو إما نار أو هواء أو ماء أو تراب' – if you affirm 'it is fire', then 'it is not air, water, or earth' follows; but denying 'it is not fire' gives nothing.
4
Practice with Islamic examples
Construct examples from fiqh or kalam to test both productive and non-productive forms using the rules above.
Ensure the disjunction is indeed exclusive of union but not exclusive of emptiness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كانت مانعة الجمع دون الخلو، فإما ذات جزأين أو أكثر، فإن كانت ذات جزأين، كقولنا : المتحرك إما جماد أو نبات فاستثناء عين كل واحد منهما ينتج نقيض الآخر على الشرط المذكور، فإنه إذا كان نباتاً فليس بجماد، وإذا كان جماداً فليس بنبات وإما استثناء النقيض من كل واحد منهما فغير مستثمر لشيء، فلو قلت لكنه ليس بجماد) لم يلزم منه أنه نبات أو ليس، ولو قلت إنه ليس بنبات لم يلزم منه أنه (جماد أو ليس). وإن كانت أكثر من ذلك وهي متباينة كقولنا كل جسم بسيط فهو إما نار أو هواء أو ماء أو تراب فاستثناء عين كل واحد منها ينتج نقيض البواقي، أو سالبة منفصلة سالبة المنع من الخلو، واستثناء النقيض لا يثمر شيئاً.
And if it is exclusive of union but not of emptiness, then it either has two parts or more. If it has two parts, like our saying: 'The moving thing is either mineral or plant'. Then the exception of the positive from each one produces the negation of the other on the mentioned condition: if it is plant, then it is not mineral; if it is mineral, then it is not plant. And as for the exception of the negation from each one, it yields nothing: if you say 'but it is not mineral', it does not follow that it is plant or not; and if you say 'it is not plant', it does not follow that it is mineral or not. And if it is more than that and they are distinct, like our saying: 'Every simple body is either fire, air, water, or earth'. Then the exception of the positive from each one produces the negation of the rest, or a negative disjunction that prevents emptiness; and the exception of the negation yields nothing.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage discusses disjunctions that are exclusive of conjunction (mani'at al-jam') but not exclusive of emptiness (la al-khuluw), meaning the parts cannot be true together but they can all be false. For a two-part case (e.g., 'moving thing is either mineral or plant'), affirming one part yields the denial of the other, but denying one part yields nothing (the other may or may not be true). For more than two parts (e.g., 'every simple body is either fire, air, water, or earth'), affirming one part yields the denial of all the rest (since they cannot coexist), but denying one part yields nothing (since they could all be false). Thus, the only productive form is affirming a part (positive exception).

Key Terms
مانعة الجمع دون الخلو
استثناء النقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a multi-part disjunction exclusive of union (e.g., 'The simple body is either fire, air, water, or earth'), if you deny that it is fire, what follows?
A) It is not air, water, or earth
B) It is either air, water, or earth
C) Nothing follows
D) The disjunction becomes invalid

Key Takeaway
Affirming a part in an exclusive-union disjunction denies the rest; denying a part is inconclusive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a valid syllogism with a two-part exclusive-union disjunction on a fiqh topic (e.g., 'The sale is either valid or void. It is valid. Therefore it is not void.') and explain why the reverse (denying one part) fails.
?
Given a three-part disjunction: 'This transaction is either a sale (bay'), rental (ijarah), or loan (qard). It is not a sale.' What can you conclude? Explain using the rule.

Inclusive Disjunction: Mani'at al-Khuluw
Fragment defining inclusive disjunction (mani'at al-khuluw).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical properties of a disjunction that is exclusive of emptiness but not of union (mani'at al-khuluw duna al-jam').

Core Idea
Mani'at al-khuluw: at least one disjunct is true, but all can be true.

This lesson introduces the second type of disjunction in the classification: exclusive of emptiness (mani'at al-khuluw). Unlike the strict exclusive disjunction which prevents both union and emptiness, this type only prevents both disjuncts from being false simultaneously. Understanding this distinction is crucial for analyzing compound syllogisms in Islamic logic, especially when evaluating legal and theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining condition
The term 'mani'at al-khuluw' means it prevents emptiness (khuluw), i.e., all parts being false. So at least one part must be true. The addition 'duna al-jam'' means it does not prevent union (jam'), i.e., all parts being true together.
'Empty' here means false: the disjunction is empty if all disjuncts are false.
2
Contrast with the exclusive type
Recall the previous type: mani'at al-jam' duna al-khuluw (exclusive of union, allowing emptiness). That type prevents all parts being true but allows all false. This new type is the opposite: it prevents all false but allows all true.
These two are complementary; together with the strict exclusive (preventing both), they form the three types of disjunction.
3
Test with the incomplete example
The passage gives: 'Either this locus is not white...' (likely continuing 'or it is black'). Both can be true: if the locus is black, it is not white and also black. So the disjunction is true when at least one part holds, even if both hold.
The example is incomplete, but the logical structure is clear: it is inclusive OR.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كانت مانعة الخلو دون الجمع، كقولنا: إما أن لا يكون هذا المحل أبيض
And if it is exclusive of emptiness but not of union, like our saying: 'Either this locus is not white'...
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage is a fragment that introduces the next type of disjunction: exclusive of emptiness (mani'at al-khuluw) but not of union, meaning at least one part must be true but all can be true simultaneously. The example given is incomplete: 'Either this locus is not white...' likely continues with another alternative such as 'or it is black' etc. The full rules of inference for this type are not provided here. This fragment appears at the end of the available pages.

Key Terms
مانعة الخلو دون الجمع (mani'at al-khuluw duna al-jam'): A disjunction that prevents all parts from being false (at least one must be true) but allows multiple parts to be true together. [Note: Standard term for inclusive disjunction; often contrasted with exclusive disjunction.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a disjunction that is exclusive of emptiness but not of union, which of the following must hold?
A) At least one disjunct is true.
B) At most one disjunct is true.
C) Exactly one disjunct is true.
D) All disjuncts are false.

Key Takeaway
Mani'at al-khuluw duna al-jam' is the inclusive disjunction: at least one part must be true, and possibly all parts are true.

Identify whether this is an exclusive or inclusive disjunction, and explain how you would apply the rule of inference.

Why Infinite Disjunctive Syllogisms Fail
Author argues that disjunctive syllogisms with infinite parts yield no conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a disjunctive syllogism is invalid because its parts are infinite and explain why no conclusion can be drawn.

Core Idea
A disjunctive syllogism with infinite parts cannot yield a conclusion.

In this passage, the author explains a key limitation of disjunctive syllogisms: when the alternatives are infinite, the syllogism becomes futile. This is a practical constraint often overlooked by beginners. Understanding this helps you avoid invalid arguments in theological and legal reasoning where infinite possibilities might be invoked.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive proposition
Find the statement that presents a choice between alternatives. In the source, the example is 'every number is either two or three or what is after that' – a disjunctive with many parts.
2
Determine if the parts are finite or infinite
Check whether the number of alternatives is limited or unlimited. The author gives the example of numbers, which have infinite ranks.
Infinite parts cannot be fully listed or conceived.
3
Recognize the impossibility of expression and conception
If the parts are infinite, they have no limit, and it is impossible to express or conceive each part individually. Thus, no disjunctive proposition with infinite parts can be formed verbally or mentally.
4
Concluding that no valid syllogism is possible
A syllogism requires two decisive propositions. Since the infinite disjunctive cannot be fully grasped, any attempt to use it in a syllogism is useless, and no conclusion can be reached.
The author notes that even partial exclusion yields a remainder that is still infinite, perpetuating the problem.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كانت الأجزاء غير متناهية كمراتب الأعداد مثلاً، فإنه لا حد لها، وما لا حدّ له فالنطق به متعذر لا بجملته بل بكل واحد من أجزائه، وكذا أيضاً تعقله فلا يكون منه قضية منفصلة غير متناهية الأجزاء لا لفظاً ولا عقلاً، وكل قياس فلا بدّ فيه من قولين جازمين فالاشتغال باستثمار مثل هذا غير مفيد ثم إنه إذا استثني منه عين أحد الأجزاء فالنتيجة إما سلب كل واحد من الأجزاء أو منفصلة سالبة من الباقي، وكل واحد من الأمرين فتعقل أجزائه بجهة التفصيل متعذر، وكذا إن استثني النقيض من البعض فالنتيجة تكون موجبة منفصلة من الباقي وتعقل أجزائها متعذر، وكذا النطق بها. ثم إن أكثر الفوائد المحصلة من جنس هذا القياس إنما هو التدرج بالاستثناء مما حصل منه إلى نتيجة واحدة وقسم واحد، كقولنا: كل عدد إما اثنان أو ثلاثة أو ما بعد ذلك كان حكمها في الاستثناء عند الاستنتاج حكم ما تقدم من مثال الحيوان والنبات والجماد.
And if the parts are infinite, like the ranks of numbers for example, then they have no limit, and what has no limit, its expression is impossible not in totality but in each one of its parts, and likewise its conception. So there cannot be from it a disjunctive proposition with infinite parts neither verbally nor conceptually. And every syllogism must have two decisive statements, so occupying oneself with extracting from such is not beneficial. Then if from it the very one of the parts is excluded, the conclusion is either the negation of each one of the parts or a negative disjunctive from the remainder, and each of the two matters, its parts are impossible to conceive in detail. Likewise if the contradictory of some is excluded, the conclusion is a positive disjunctive from the remainder, and its parts are impossible to conceive, and likewise its expression. Then most of the benefits obtained from this kind of syllogism is only the gradual exclusion from what has been obtained from it to a single conclusion and a single division, like our saying: every number is either two or three or what is after that, its ruling in exclusion at inference is the same as what preceded from the example of animal, plant, and mineral.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage explains why disjunctive syllogisms with infinite parts (e.g., numbers) are impossible. The author argues that since infinite parts have no limit, they cannot be fully expressed or conceived. A valid syllogism requires two decisive propositions, but an infinite disjunctive cannot be fully grasped, making extraction of a conclusion futile. Even if one part or its contradictory is excluded, the resulting conclusion (a negative or positive disjunctive of the remainder) would also involve infinite parts, which cannot be conceptualized or expressed in detail. However, the author notes a practical benefit: using gradual exclusion to reach a single conclusion, as in the example of numbers (2, 3, etc.) – but this only works if the parts are finite and grouped into one result.

Key Terms
أجزاء غير متناهية
قضية منفصلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author say a disjunctive syllogism with infinite parts is not beneficial?
A) Because the premises are always false.
B) Because the parts cannot be fully expressed or conceived.
C) Because no conclusion can ever be drawn.
D) Because it leads to a contradiction.

Key Takeaway
A disjunctive syllogism with infinite parts is invalid because the alternatives cannot be fully expressed or conceived, making any conclusion impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Exclusive Disjunction with Two Parts
Author defines rules for exclusive disjunctive syllogisms with two parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the valid inferences in an exclusive disjunctive syllogism with two parts.

Core Idea
Affirming one part negates the other; negating one part yields no conclusion.

The author explains the rules for exclusive disjunctions that do not exhaust all possibilities. Understanding these rules is crucial for logical reasoning where options are mutually exclusive but not necessarily comprehensive. In this lesson, we focus on the case with exactly two parts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of disjunction
Determine if the disjunction is exclusive (مانعة الجمع) and has exactly two parts. The author specifies 'ذات جزأين' (having two parts). Check if the parts are mutually exclusive.
Exclusive disjunction means the parts cannot all be true together, but they may all be false.
2
Extract the two parts
List the two disjuncts from the example: 'جماد' (mineral) and 'نبات' (plant). These are the options in the exclusive disjunction.
3
Apply the rule for affirmation (استثناء العين)
If you affirm one part, you can conclude the negation of the other part. For example, if it is a plant, it is not a mineral; if it is a mineral, it is not a plant.
This is because they cannot both be true.
4
Apply the rule for negation (استثناء النقيض)
If you negate one part, you cannot conclude anything about the other. For example, saying 'it is not a mineral' does not mean it is a plant; it could be animal or something else.
This is because the disjunction is not exhaustive.
5
Test with the provided example
Apply the rules to the example of the moving thing being mineral or plant. Verify that affirmation of one leads to negation of the other, and negation of one yields no conclusion.
The author explicitly states: 'ولو قلت لكنه ليس بجماد لم يلزم منه أنه نبات أو ليس'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كانت مانعة الجمع دون الخلو، فإما ذات جزأين أو أكثر، فإن كانت ذات جزأين، كقولنا: المتحرك إما جماد أو نبات فاستثناء عين كل واحد منهما ينتج نقيض الآخر على الشرط المذكور، فإنه إذا كان نباتاً فليس بجماد، وإذا كان جماداً فليس بنبات وإما استثناء النقيض من كل واحد منهما فغير مستثمر لشيء، فلو قلت لكنه ليس بجماد) لم يلزم منه أنه نبات أو ليس، ولو قلت إنه ليس بنبات لم يلزم منه أنه (جماد أو ليس). وإن كانت أكثر من ذلك وهي متباينة كقولنا كل جسم بسيط فهو إما نار أو هواء أو ماء أو تراب فاستثناء عين كل واحد منها ينتج نقيض البواقي، أو سالبة منفصلة سالبة المنع من الخلو، واستثناء النقيض لا يثمر شيئاً.
And if it is exclusive without exhaustive, then it is either with two parts or more. If it has two parts, like our saying: the moving thing is either mineral or plant, then excluding the very one of each of them produces the contradictory of the other on the mentioned condition. For if it is a plant, it is not a mineral; and if it is a mineral, it is not a plant. And excluding the contradictory of each of them yields nothing. So if you say 'but it is not a mineral', it does not follow that it is a plant or not; and if you say 'it is not a plant', it does not follow that it is a mineral or not. And if there are more than that and they are distinct, like our saying: every simple body is either fire or air or water or earth, then excluding the very one of each of them produces the contradictory of the rest, or a negative disjunctive denying exclusivity, and excluding the contradictory yields nothing.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage explains the rules for exclusive disjunctive syllogisms (مانعة الجمع دون الخلو), where the parts cannot coexist but may both be false. For two parts, affirming one part (استثناء العين) entails the negation of the other, because if one holds, the other cannot. For example, if a moving thing is a plant, it cannot be a mineral. However, negating one part (استثناء النقيض) yields no conclusion, because the other part may still be false; the disjunction does not guarantee that one of them must be true. For three or more distinct parts (e.g., the four elements), affirming one part negates all the others, because if one element is present, the others are excluded. Negating any part again yields nothing, as the remaining parts are not necessarily exhaustive.

Key Terms
مانعة الجمع
استثناء العين
استثناء النقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In an exclusive disjunction (مانعة الجمع دون الخلو) with two parts, what follows from affirming one part?
A) The other part is affirmed.
B) The other part is negated.
C) Nothing follows.
D) Both parts are negated.

Key Takeaway
In a two-part exclusive disjunction, affirming one part negates the other, but negating one part yields no conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Exclusive Disjunction with Multiple Parts
Author defines rules for exclusive disjunctive syllogisms with more than two parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule of affirming one part to negate all others in an exclusive disjunction with multiple distinct parts.

Core Idea
Affirming one of many mutually exclusive parts negates all the rest; negating any part is inconclusive.

The author extends the discussion to exclusive disjunctions with more than two parts. The same principle holds: affirmation of one part forces negation of all others, but negation of a single part does not affirm any specific other. This is illustrated with the example of the four elements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the number of parts
Confirm the disjunction has more than two distinct parts. The author gives an example of four parts: fire, air, water, earth.
The parts must be distinct (متباينة) and mutually exclusive.
2
List all the parts
Enumerate all disjuncts: fire (نار), air (هواء), water (ماء), earth (تراب).
3
Apply the affirmation rule (استثناء العين)
If one part is affirmed, you can conclude the negation of all other parts. For example, if it is fire, then it is not air, not water, not earth.
The author says: 'فاستثناء عين كل واحد منها ينتج نقيض البواقي'.
4
Apply the negation rule (استثناء النقيض)
If you negate any single part, you cannot conclude anything about the remaining parts. They could still be true or false.
The author explicitly states: 'واستثناء النقيض لا يثمر شيئاً'.
5
Work through the example
Take the disjunction of simple bodies. Affirm it is fire; then it is not air, water, or earth. Negate it is fire; that does not tell you which element it is.
This rule applies regardless of the number of parts, as long as the disjunction is exclusive.

And if it is exclusive without exhaustive, then it is either with two parts or more. If it has two parts, like our saying: the moving thing is either mineral or plant, then excluding the very one of each of them produces the contradictory of the other on the mentioned condition. For if it is a plant, it is not a mineral; and if it is a mineral, it is not a plant. And excluding the contradictory of each of them yields nothing. So if you say 'but it is not a mineral', it does not follow that it is a plant or not; and if you say 'it is not a plant', it does not follow that it is a mineral or not. And if there are more than that and they are distinct, like our saying: every simple body is either fire or air or water or earth, then excluding the very one of each of them produces the contradictory of the rest, or a negative disjunctive denying exclusivity, and excluding the contradictory yields nothing.

This passage explains the rules for exclusive disjunctive syllogisms (مانعة الجمع دون الخلو), where the parts cannot coexist but may both be false. However, negating one part (استثناء النقيض) yields no conclusion, because the other part may still be false; the disjunction does not guarantee that one of them must be true. For three or more distinct parts (e.g., the four elements), affirming one part negates all the others, because if one element is present, the others are excluded. Negating any part again yields nothing, as the remaining parts are not necessarily exhaustive.

Exhaustive Disjunctive Syllogisms: The Rule of Man'at al-Khuluw
Author defines rules for exhaustive disjunctive syllogisms where parts cover all possibilities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the valid inference when negating one part of an exhaustive disjunction in a logical argument.

Core Idea
Negating a part in an exhaustive disjunction affirms the other; affirming a part yields no conclusion.

This passage from Daqa’iq al-Haqa’iq clarifies the rules for exhaustive disjunctive syllogisms (مانعة الخلو دون الجمع). Understanding these rules is crucial for valid reasoning in Islamic kalam and fiqh debates, where such disjunctions often appear.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunction type
Determine if the disjunction is exclusive (man'at al-jam') or exhaustive (man'at al-khuluw). This passage focuses on the latter, where at least one part must be true but both can be true.
Key term: مانعة الخلو دون الجمع.
2
Parse the disjuncts
Break down the two parts of the disjunction. For example, 'either this act is not wajib or it is not haram' – the disjuncts are 'not wajib' and 'not haram'.
3
Apply the negation rule
If you negate one part (i.e., affirm its contradictory), the other part follows. If you affirm one part as it is, nothing follows.
The author's example: negating 'not white' (affirming 'white') yields 'not black'.
4
Test with Islamic examples
Affirm 'wajib' (negating 'not wajib') to conclude 'not haram'. Affirm 'not wajib' yields no conclusion, as both 'not wajib' and 'not haram' could be true (e.g., an act that is mustahabb).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كانت مانعة الخلو دون الجمع، كقولنا: إما أن لا يكون هذا المحل أبيض وإما أن لا يكون أسود فاستثناء النقيض من كل واحد منهما ينتج عين الآخر، فإنك إذا قلت لكنه أبيض فإنه نقيض قولنا أنه لا أبيض ويلزم منه أنه ليس بأسود وهو عين الجزء الآخر، وكذا بالعكس، وليس يلزم من استثناء العين شيء، فإنك لو قلت لكنه ليس بأبيض لم يلزم منه أنه ليس بأسود لجواز أن يكون أسود، ولا أنه أسود لجواز أن لا يكون أسود.
And if it is exhaustive without exclusive, like our saying: either this place is not white or it is not black, then excluding the contradictory of each of them produces the very other. For if you say 'but it is white', it is the contradictory of our saying 'it is not white', and it follows that it is not black, which is the very other part. And likewise the opposite. And nothing follows from excluding the very one, for if you say 'but it is not white', it does not follow that it is not black, because it could be black, nor that it is black, because it could not be black.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage covers exhaustive disjunctions (مانعة الخلو دون الجمع), where at least one part must be true but both could be true. The example given is 'either this place is not white or it is not black' – these two negations cannot both be false (since if it is white, it is not not-white) but they can both be true if it is, say, red. The rule: negating one part (استثناء النقيض) affirms the other. For instance, if you affirm 'it is white', this contradicts 'it is not white', so the other part 'it is not black' follows. However, affirming one part (استثناء العين) yields nothing, because both could be true: if you say 'it is not white', it could still be black or not black, so no conclusion is forced.

Key Terms
مانعة الخلو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In an exhaustive disjunction (مانعة الخلو دون الجمع) where the parts are 'not wajib' and 'not haram', what follows from affirming that the act is wajib?
A) It is haram.
B) It is not haram.
C) It is wajib.
D) Nothing follows.

Key Takeaway
In an exhaustive disjunction, only by negating one part can you validly infer the other; affirming a part gives no conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the disjunction 'Either this act is not wajib or it is not haram', determine what follows if you affirm that the act is wajib.
?

Why Negative Disjunctive Syllogisms Are Invalid
Author explains that negative disjunctive syllogisms produce no valid conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a negative disjunctive proposition and explain why no conclusion follows from it.

Core Idea
A negated disjunction tells you what is not the case, but not what is; therefore no valid inference.

In the previous lesson, you learned how positive disjunctive syllogisms can yield valid conclusions when one disjunct is excluded. However, what happens when the entire disjunctive premise is negated? The classical logicians meticulously show that such negative disjunctive propositions produce no logical inference at all. In this lesson, we will examine the author's analysis of three types of negative disjunctives and understand why they are all invalid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of negative disjunctive propositions
Locate in the passage the three cases: (1) negation of exclusive+exhaustive, (2) negation of exclusive only, (3) negation of exhaustive only.
These correspond to negations of the three disjunction types.
2
Understand why negating the conditions does not entail anything
For each type, the author shows that negating the impossibility of combination does not mean combination occurs, and negating the impossibility of missing both does not mean one must be present.
This is the core logical insight.
3
Apply the reasoning to an Islamic example
Take the negative disjunction: 'It is not the case that either this action is wajib or it is mubah.' From this, you cannot conclude it is wajib nor mubah. Check this for each type.
This demonstrates practical invalidity.
4
Generalize the principle
Conclude that any negative disjunctive premise is sterile; it gives no information about the truth of the disjuncts.
This is a universal rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كانت المنفصلة سالبة فإما سالبة المنع من الجمع والخلو وإما سالبة المنع من الجمع دون الخلو. أما سالبة المنع من الخلو دون الجمع، فإن كانت سالبة المنع من الجمع والخلو ولا إنتاج، وسواء استثني العين أو النقيض، أما إذا استثنى العين فلا يلزم منه عين الجزء والأخر؛ إذ ليس يلزم من سلب امتناع الجمع الجمع، ولا نقيضه الآخر، إذ ليس يلزم من سلب امتناع الجمع أن لا يجتمعا، وعلى هذا فلا يخفى الحال في استثناء النقيض أيضاً. وأما إن كانت سالبة المنع من الجمع دون الخلو فلا إنتاج، وسواء استثنى العين أو النقيض، أما إذا استثنى العين، فلجواز أن يكون المستثنى أعم من الجزء الآخر، وذلك كما لو قيل ليس إما أن يكون هذا إنساناً وإما حيواناً فإنه ليس يلزم من قولنا: أنه حيوان أن يكون إنساناً أو ليس. وأما إذا استثنى النقيض، فلجواز أن يكون المستثنى أخص من الجزء الآخر، كما لو قلت لكنه ليس بإنسان فإنه لا يلزم منه أنه حيوان أو ليس بحيوان. وإن كانت سالبة المنع من الخلو دون الجمع فلا إنتاج أيضاً، أما إذا استثنى العين فلأنه لا يلزم منه عين الجزء الآخر، فإنه لا يلزم من سلب امتناع الخلو امتناع الجمع، وعلى هذا فلا يخفى الحال في استثناء النقيض أيضاً.
And if the disjunction is negative, then it is either a negative of the prevention of combination and emptiness, or a negative of the prevention of combination without emptiness. As for the negative of prevention of emptiness without combination, if it is a negative of prevention of combination and emptiness, there is no production, whether the very one or the contradictory is excluded. For if the very one is excluded, the very other part does not follow; because from negating the impossibility of combination, the combination does not follow, nor its contradictory the other. Likewise for excluding the contradictory. And if it is a negative of prevention of combination without emptiness, there is no production, whether the very one or the contradictory is excluded. For if the very one is excluded, because the excluded may be more general than the other part, as if it is said 'not either this is a human or an animal', then from our saying 'it is an animal' it does not follow that it is a human or not. And if the contradictory is excluded, because the excluded may be more specific than the other part, as if you say 'but it is not a human', then it does not follow that it is an animal or not an animal. And if it is a negative of prevention of emptiness without combination, there is no production either. For if the very one is excluded, because the very other part does not follow; for from negating the prevention of emptiness, the prevention of combination does not follow. Likewise for excluding the contradictory.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage discusses negative disjunctive syllogisms, where the disjunction itself is negated. The author divides them into three types: (1) negative of both exclusive and exhaustive (سالبة المنع من الجمع والخلو), (2) negative of exclusive only (سالبة المنع من الجمع دون الخلو), and (3) negative of exhaustive only (سالبة المنع من الخلو دون الجمع). In all three, no valid conclusion follows regardless of whether one affirms or negates a part. The reasoning: negating the exclusivity condition does not establish that the parts actually coexist, nor that one is false. Similarly, when the negation involves a more general or more specific term, the inference fails. For example, 'it is not the case that something is either a human or an animal' – from 'it is an animal', one cannot conclude it is human or not, because it could be a non-human animal. Thus, negative disjunctive premises yield no logical inference.

Key Terms
منفصلة سالبة (munfaṣila sāliba): A disjunctive proposition that is negated, i.e., 'not (either A or B)'. [Note: Standard usage; the discussion here is unique in detailing the invalidity of such syllogisms.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) They always produce a valid conclusion.
B) They are valid only if the disjunction is exclusive.
C) They never produce a valid conclusion.
D) They are valid only if one excludes the contradictory.

Key Takeaway
Negative disjunctive syllogisms are invalid in all their forms: they yield no conclusion whether you affirm or negate a part.

The Syllogism

8 min
No Conclusion from Exception in Negative Disjunctives
Author enumerates types of negative disjunctives and states no conclusion follows from exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify the three types of negative disjunctive propositions and explain why exception fails to produce a conclusion in each type.

Core Idea
Negating exclusivity breaks the link between disjuncts, making exception useless.

In the previous lesson, we learned how exception works in affirmative disjunctives: excepting one part affirms the other, and excepting the contradictory of one part gives the contradictory of the other. But what happens when the disjunctive itself is negative? The author now turns to negative disjunctives, categorizing them into three types based on which exclusivity condition is negated. He then shows that in each type, whether you except the same part or its contradictory, no necessary conclusion follows. This is a critical nuance in the theory of disjunctive syllogisms.

Guided Path
1
Recall the three exclusivity conditions
Man' al-jam' means both parts cannot be true simultaneously; man' al-khuluw means both cannot be false simultaneously. Their combination yields complete exclusivity. Negative disjunctives negate one or both of these conditions.
These conditions are the backbone of disjunctive syllogisms.
2
Identify the three subtypes of negative disjunctives
The passage lists: (a) negative of man' al-jam' wa al-khuluw, (b) negative of man' al-jam' duna al-khuluw, (c) negative of man' al-khuluw duna al-jam'. Each negates a different aspect of exclusivity.
These are standard classifications in later logic texts.
3
Test exception of the same part ('ayn) in each subtype
Take the example 'Not either this is a human or an animal' (type b). If you except 'it is an animal', no necessary conclusion follows because the excepted term may be more general. Similarly for other types: negating exclusivity does not force a relation.
The example shows that excepting a part doesn't imply anything about the other part.
4
Test exception of the contradictory (naqid) in each subtype
From the same disjunctive, if you except 'it is not a human', no conclusion follows because the excepted term may be more specific. The pattern holds across all subtypes.
The reasoning is symmetric: negation of exclusivity removes the logical link.
5
Generalize the rule: No production in negative disjunctives
The underlying reason is that when you negate the exclusivity condition, you remove the constraint that forces a relationship between the parts. Hence, exception cannot produce a necessary conclusion.
This is a key difference from affirmative disjunctives.

وإما أن لا يكون (أسود فاستثناء النقيض من كل واحد منهما ينتج عين الآخر، فإنك إذا قلت لكنه أبيض فإنه نقيض قولنا أنه لا) (أبيض ويلزم منه أنه ليس بأسود) وهو عين الجزء الآخر، وكذا بالعكس، وليس يلزم من استثناء العين شيء، فإنك لو قلت لكنه ليس بأبيض لم يلزم منه أنه ليس بأسود لجواز أن يكون أسود، ولا أنه (أسود) لجواز أن لا يكون (أسود). وأما إن كانت المنفصلة سالبة فإما سالبة المنع من الجمع والخلو وإما سالبة المنع من الجمع دون الخلو. أما سالبة المنع من الخلو دون الجمع، فإن كانت سالبة المنع من الجمع والخلو ولا إنتاج، وسواء استثني العين أو النقيض، أما إذا استثنى العين فلا يلزم منه عين الجزء والآخر؛ إذ ليس يلزم من سلب امتناع الجمع الجمع، ولا نقيضه الآخر، إذ ليس يلزم من سلب امتناع الجمع أن لا يجتمعا، وعلى هذا فلا يخفى الحال في استثناء النقيض أيضاً. وأما إن كانت سالبة المنع من الجمع دون الخلو فلا إنتاج، وسواء استثنى العين أو النقيض، أما إذا استثنى العين، فلجواز أن يكون المستثنى أعم من الجزء الآخر، وذلك كما لو قيل ليس إما أن يكون هذا إنساناً وإما حيواناً فإنه ليس يلزم من قولنا: أنه (حيوان أن يكون إنساناً أو ليس). وأما إذا استثنى النقيض، فلجواز أن يكون المستثنى أخص من الجزء الآخر، كما لو قلت لكنه ليس بإنسان فإنه لا يلزم منه أنه حيوان أو ليس بحيوان. وإن كانت سالبة المنع من الخلو دون الجمع فلا إنتاج أيضاً، أما إذا استثنى العين فلأنه لا يلزم منه عين الجزء الآخر، فإنه لا يلزم من سلب امتناع الخلو امتناع الجمع، وعلى هذا فلا يخفى الحال في استثناء النقيض أيضاً.
And as for not being (black), then exception of the contradictory of each one of them produces the same of the other. For if you say 'but it is white', then it is the contradictory of our statement 'it is not white' and it follows from it that it is not black, which is the same of the other part, and similarly in reverse. And nothing follows from exception of the same. For if you said 'but it is not white', it does not follow that it is not black because it is possible that it is black, nor that it is black because it is possible that it is not black. And if the disjunctive is negative, then either it is negative of prevention of conjunction and void, or negative of prevention of conjunction without void. As for the negative of prevention of void without conjunction, if it is negative of prevention of conjunction and void, there is no production, whether the same or the contradictory is excepted. As for if the same is excepted, then the same of the part and the other does not follow; for the negation of the impossibility of conjunction does not necessitate conjunction, nor its contradictory the other, for the negation of the impossibility of conjunction does not necessitate that they do not coexist. And accordingly the situation regarding exception of the contradictory is not hidden. And if it is negative of prevention of conjunction without void, there is no production, whether the same or the contradictory is excepted. As for if the same is excepted, because the excepted may be more general than the other part. This is as if it is said 'not either this is a human or an animal', for it does not follow from our statement 'it is an animal' that it is a human or not. And if the contradictory is excepted, because the excepted may be more specific than the other part, as if you said 'but it is not a human', it does not follow that it is an animal or not an animal. And if it is negative of prevention of void without conjunction, there is no production either. As for if the same is excepted, because the same of the other part does not follow; for the negation of the impossibility of void does not necessitate the impossibility of conjunction. And accordingly the situation regarding exception of the contradictory is not hidden.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the rules of exception (istithna') in negative disjunctive syllogisms (al-munfasila al-saliba). The author begins by recalling the case where the disjunctive is affirmative, then focuses on negative disjunctives. He classifies negative disjunctives into three types based on the kind of exclusivity: (1) negative of man' al-jam' wa al-khuluw (i.e., negating that the two parts are mutually exclusive in both conjunction and void), (2) negative of man' al-jam' duna al-khuluw (negating mutual exclusivity in conjunction only), and (3) negative of man' al-khuluw duna al-jam' (negating mutual exclusivity in void only). For each type, he demonstrates that whether one excepts the same part ('ayn) or its contradictory (naqid), no necessary conclusion follows. The key reason is that negating an exclusive relation does not establish a positive link between the parts. The author uses the example of 'human' and 'animal' to illustrate: in the negative disjunctive 'Not either this is a human or an animal', excepting 'animal' does not yield 'human' or its negation, because the excepted term is more general. Similarly, excepting 'not human' does not yield 'animal' or its negation because the excepted is more specific. Thus, the passage reinforces that valid syllogistic production requires positive exclusivity conditions.

المنع من الجمع والخلو
استثناء العين

A) It must be a human.

C) Either it is a human or it is not a human.

Key Takeaway
In negative disjunctives, no matter what you except, no conclusion follows because the exclusivity condition is absent.

How Disjunctive Syllogisms Hide Categorical Syllogisms
Author demonstrates how disjunctive syllogisms reduce to conjunctive ones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to extract the implicit categorical syllogism that underlies any given disjunctive syllogism.

Core Idea
Every disjunctive syllogism hides a conjunctive (categorical) syllogism in the mind.

The author demonstrates that a disjunctive syllogism like 'Every number is either even or odd; but it is even; therefore it is not odd' is only valid because we mentally assume a conditional premise: 'If even, then not odd'. This conditional, when expressed as a categorical proposition ('All even numbers are not odd'), forms the basis of a categorical syllogism. In this lesson, we will practice uncovering that hidden categorical argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjunctive premise and the exception
Locate the disjunctive (munfasil) premise and the part that is excepted (mustathna). In the even/odd example, the disjunctive premise is 'Every number is either even or odd' and the exception is 'it is even'.
The conclusion is the opposite of the other disjunct.
2
Recognize the implicit conditional premise
The validity of the inference depends on an implicit conditional (muttasil) that connects the excepted disjunct to the negation of the other. Here, the mind assumes 'if it is even, then it is not odd'.
This conditional is often not stated explicitly.
3
Convert the conditional into a categorical proposition
Rewrite the conditional as a universal categorical statement. 'If even, then not odd' becomes 'All even numbers are not odd' (an E proposition in traditional logic).
The categorical form makes the underlying syllogism explicit.
4
Form the categorical syllogism and check validity
Combine the categorical premise with the exception (as minor premise) to deduce the conclusion. For example: 'All even numbers are not odd. This number is even. Therefore, this number is not odd.' This is a valid first-figure syllogism (Celarent style).
This shows that the disjunctive syllogism reduces to a categorical syllogism.
