⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني في تعريف ما قيل من أن القياسات الاستثنائية لا تتم بالاقترانية وتحقيق القول فيه. فنقول: قد ظهر انقسام الاستثنائي إلى المتصل والمنفصل، فإن لم يتم المتصل بغير الاقتراني فظاهر أن المنفصل لا يتم إلا به لضرورة أن المتصل لا يتم دون المتصل، فإنك إذا قلت في مانعة الجمع والخلو كل عدد إما زوج وإما فرد ثم قلت لكنه زوج فإنه وإن لم يلزم منه أنه ليس بفرد فإنما هو بتقدير قياس ذهني متصل وهو أنه كل ما كان العدد زوجاً فليس بفرد فإذا قيل إنه (زوج) لزم أنه (ليس بفرد)، وكذا إذا قيل إنه ليس بزوج) فلزوم (الفرد) وإن كان فليس أيضاً إلا بتقدير قياس متصل في الذهن ، وهو أنه كل ما كان العدد ليس بزوج فهو فرد) فإذا قيل إنه ليس بزوج لزم أنه (فرد) وعلى هذا في مانعة الجمع دون الخلو، ومانعة الخلو دون الجمع. وعند هذا فنقول المتصل إما أن يكون المستثنى عين المقدم من الشرطية أو نقيض تاليها، فإن كان الأول فالذي تكلّفه بعض المحققين في بيان افتقاره إلى الاقتراني أن قال: لو كان المقدم بيناً لكان التالي له بيّناً، ولا معنى للقياس؛ لكونه لا يبين غير الخفي، فإذن يجب أن يكون غير بين وبيانه إذ ذاك لا يكون بغير الاقتراني قطعاً؛ للتسلسل، وهو تغافل في التحقيق إذ ربما كان الشيء بيناً بنفسه ولازمه غير بين إلا بواسطة لزومه لذلك الشيء البين بنفسه، وليس معنى كون الشيء بيناً بنفسه أنه بيّن بتوسط لزومه للبيّن، وإلا صارت الأشياء كلها بيّنة بأنفسها؛ لضرورة انتهائها في البيان إلى ما هو بين بنفسه، ثم كيف وأنه ربما كان الشيء بيناً ولزوم التالي ليس بيناً إلا بحجة، ثم وإن سلمت لا بُدّية كون المقدم مشكوكاً فيه فإنما يجب بيانه بالاقتراني، أو بما هو أقل في البيان إليه، بل لعله قد يتبين بما هو في نفسه بين لا بقياس، ويكون لزومه لذلك البين في البيان على نحو لزوم التالي للمقدم عند كون
The second chapter: On explaining what has been said that conditional syllogisms are not completed without categorical [syllogisms] and verifying the statement about it. We say: The division of the conditional into conjunctive and disjunctive has appeared. For if the conjunctive is not completed without a categorical, then it is apparent that the disjunctive is not completed except through it due to the necessity that the conjunctive is not completed without the conjunctive. For if you say in the exclusive of conjunction and void 'every number is either even or odd', then you say 'but it is even', even if it does not follow from it that it is not odd, it is only by assuming a mental conjunctive syllogism: 'every number that is even is not odd'. So when it is said that it is even, it follows that it is not odd. Similarly, if it is said that it is not even, then the necessary consequence of odd, even if it is also only by assuming a mental conjunctive syllogism: 'every number that is not even is odd'. So when it is said that it is not even, it follows that it is odd. And similarly for the exclusive of conjunction without void and exclusive of void without conjunction. At this point we say: The conjunctive is either such that the excepted is the same as the antecedent of the conditional or the contradictory of its consequent. If it is the first, then what some investigators undertook in explaining its need for a categorical is that they said: If the antecedent were self-evident, then its consequent would be self-evident, and there would be no meaning to a syllogism because it does not clarify anything other than the obscure. Therefore, it must be not self-evident, and its clarification then is certainly not without a categorical, due to infinite regress. And this is negligence in verification, for sometimes a thing is self-evident in itself and its necessary consequent is not self-evident except through the intermediacy of its necessity to that self-evident thing. And the meaning of a thing being self-evident in itself is not that it is evident through the intermediacy of its necessity to the evident; otherwise all things would become self-evident in themselves, by necessity of their termination in evidence to what is self-evident in itself. Then how [can it be]? For sometimes a thing is self-evident and the entailment of the consequent is not evident except by a proof. Then even if we grant the necessity of the antecedent being doubtful, it must be clarified by a categorical, or by something less in clarification to it. Rather, perhaps it may become evident by what is in itself evident without a syllogism, and its necessity to that evident [thing] in clarification is on the model of the necessity of the consequent to the antecedent when it is...
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument for the claim that all conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i), whether conjunctive (muttasil) or disjunctive (munfasil), ultimately depend on categorical syllogisms (qiyas iqtirani) for their validity. The author begins by noting the division of conditionals into conjunctive and disjunctive. He then shows that disjunctive syllogisms are incomplete without an underlying conjunctive syllogism. For example, the disjunctive 'Every number is either even or odd; but it is even; therefore it is not odd' is only valid because the mind implicitly assumes a conjunctive premise: 'Every number that is even is not odd'. The same reasoning applies to the exclusive of conjunction without void and void without conjunction. Next, the author considers a challenge raised by some investigators regarding the need for a categorical when the excepted term is the same as the antecedent of a conditional. They argued that if the antecedent were self-evident, the consequent would be self-evident too, making the syllogism pointless; therefore the antecedent must be doubtful, and clarifying it requires a categorical syllogism to avoid infinite regress. The author refutes this by pointing out that a thing can be self-evident in itself while its necessary consequent is not self-evident without an intermediary. He also argues that the investigator's reasoning would lead to all things being self-evident, which is false. Moreover, the antecedent may be self-evident yet the entailment of the consequent still requires a proof. The passage thus defends the indispensable role of categorical syllogisms in conditional reasoning.

Key Terms
القياس الاستثنائي
القياس الاقتراني
المقدم
المنفصل
المتصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the disjunctive premise itself is a categorical proposition.
B) Because the mind must assume the conditional 'If even, then not odd', which reduces to a categorical universal.
C) Because the conclusion is explicitly stated in the disjunctive premise.
D) Because the exception is of the contradictory of the consequent.

Key Takeaway
Disjunctive syllogisms are not self-sufficient; they derive their validity from an implicit categorical syllogism that must be made explicit for rigorous analysis.

Refuting the Claim That the Antecedent Must Be Doubtful
Author critiques the argument that conditional syllogisms need a categorical due to the antecedent being doubtful.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to refute the argument that a conditional syllogism requires the antecedent to be doubtful in order to need a categorical proof.

Core Idea
Self-evident premises can have non-evident consequents, so a syllogism is still necessary.

Some investigators argued that if the antecedent of a conditional is self-evident, its consequent would also be self-evident, making the syllogism pointless. Therefore, they claimed, the antecedent must be doubtful, and clarifying it requires a categorical syllogism to avoid infinite regress. The author refutes this by showing that a self-evident thing can have a consequent that is not self-evident, and that the investigator's reasoning leads to an absurd conclusion. In this lesson, we will dissect this critical exchange.

Guided Path
1
Reconstruct the investigator's argument
Identify the claim: If the antecedent (muqaddam) is self-evident (bayyin bi-nafsihi), then the consequent (tali) is also self-evident, so no syllogism is needed. Hence, in a conditional syllogism where the excepted part is the antecedent, the antecedent must be non-evident, and its clarification requires a categorical syllogism, otherwise infinite regress.
This is a conditional argument: if P then Q; not Q; therefore not P (modus tollens).
2
Find the author's counterexample
The author points out that sometimes a thing is self-evident in itself but its necessary consequent is not self-evident except through the intermediacy of its necessity to that self-evident thing. For instance, 'the whole is greater than its part' is self-evident, but the consequent 'this specific whole is greater than this specific part' may not be self-evident without additional reasoning.
The author uses logical terminology: 'lazim' (necessary consequent) and 'bayyin bi-nafsihi' (self-evident).
3
Explain the reductio ad absurdum
The author argues that if the investigator's definition were correct, then everything would become self-evident, because all chains of evidence ultimately terminate in self-evident truths. This is absurd, so the investigator's premise is false.
This is a common argumentative strategy: reduce the opponent's position to an obviously false conclusion.
4
Summarize the author's positive view
The author concludes that the antecedent may indeed be self-evident, yet the entailment of the consequent may still require a proof (maybe categorical, maybe not). The necessity of a categorical syllogism is not based on the antecedent's being doubtful, but on the need to make the implication explicit.
The author does not deny the need for categorical syllogisms; he only rejects the investigator's flawed justification.

The second chapter: On explaining what has been said that conditional syllogisms are not completed without categorical [syllogisms] and verifying the statement about it. We say: The division of the conditional into conjunctive and disjunctive has appeared. For if the conjunctive is not completed without a categorical, then it is apparent that the disjunctive is not completed except through it due to the necessity that the conjunctive is not completed without the conjunctive. For if you say in the exclusive of conjunction and void 'every number is either even or odd', then you say 'but it is even', even if it does not follow from it that it is not odd, it is only by assuming a mental conjunctive syllogism: 'every number that is even is not odd'. So when it is said that it is even, it follows that it is not odd. Similarly, if it is said that it is not even, then the necessary consequence of odd, even if it is also only by assuming a mental conjunctive syllogism: 'every number that is not even is odd'. So when it is said that it is not even, it follows that it is odd. And similarly for the exclusive of conjunction without void and exclusive of void without conjunction. At this point we say: The conjunctive is either such that the excepted is the same as the antecedent of the conditional or the contradictory of its consequent. If it is the first, then what some investigators undertook in explaining its need for a categorical is that they said: If the antecedent were self-evident, then its consequent would be self-evident, and there would be no meaning to a syllogism because it does not clarify anything other than the obscure. Therefore, it must be not self-evident, and its clarification then is certainly not without a categorical, due to infinite regress. And this is negligence in verification, for sometimes a thing is self-evident in itself and its necessary consequent is not self-evident except through the intermediacy of its necessity to that self-evident thing. And the meaning of a thing being self-evident in itself is not that it is evident through the intermediacy of its necessity to the evident; otherwise all things would become self-evident in themselves, by necessity of their termination in evidence to what is self-evident in itself. Then how [can it be]? For sometimes a thing is self-evident and the entailment of the consequent is not evident except by a proof. Then even if we grant the necessity of the antecedent being doubtful, it must be clarified by a categorical, or by something less in clarification to it. Rather, perhaps it may become evident by what is in itself evident without a syllogism, and its necessity to that evident [thing] in clarification is on the model of the necessity of the consequent to the antecedent when it is...
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage is a detailed argument for the claim that all conditional syllogisms (qiyas istithna'i), whether conjunctive (muttasil) or disjunctive (munfasil), ultimately depend on categorical syllogisms (qiyas iqtirani) for their validity. The author begins by noting the division of conditionals into conjunctive and disjunctive. He then shows that disjunctive syllogisms are incomplete without an underlying conjunctive syllogism. For example, the disjunctive 'Every number is either even or odd; but it is even; therefore it is not odd' is only valid because the mind implicitly assumes a conjunctive premise: 'Every number that is even is not odd'. The same reasoning applies to the exclusive of conjunction without void and void without conjunction. Next, the author considers a challenge raised by some investigators regarding the need for a categorical when the excepted term is the same as the antecedent of a conditional. They argued that if the antecedent were self-evident, the consequent would be self-evident too, making the syllogism pointless; therefore the antecedent must be doubtful, and clarifying it requires a categorical syllogism to avoid infinite regress. The author refutes this by pointing out that a thing can be self-evident in itself while its necessary consequent is not self-evident without an intermediary. He also argues that the investigator's reasoning would lead to all things being self-evident, which is false. Moreover, the antecedent may be self-evident yet the entailment of the consequent still requires a proof. The passage thus defends the indispensable role of categorical syllogisms in conditional reasoning.

The fact that a premise is self-evident does not eliminate the need for syllogistic reasoning to derive its necessary consequents; the investigator's argument to the contrary is based on a flawed understanding of self-evidence.

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
Reliance of Disjunctive Syllogisms on Conditional Ones
Author analyzes whether exclusive syllogisms require conjunctive ones, critiques a scholar's absolute claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the dependency of disjunctive syllogisms on conditional syllogisms and evaluate the claim that all exclusive syllogisms necessarily require conjunctive ones.

Core Idea
Disjunctive syllogisms are not self-sufficient; they depend on underlying conditionals, but those conditionals may be clear without conjunctive proofs.

In this passage, the author examines the relationship between exclusive (istithnā'ī) and conjunctive (iqtirānī) syllogisms. He first shows that disjunctive syllogisms require a mental conditional to yield a conclusion. He then critiques the view that conditional syllogisms always need a conjunctive syllogism to establish their premises. By the end, you will see how the necessity of conjunctive syllogisms is not absolute but contingent on the clarity of the terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the logical structure of the disjunctive inference
The author gives the example: 'Every number is either even or odd; but it is even; therefore it is not odd.' Recognize that the conclusion does not follow from the disjunctive premise and exception alone; it requires an additional conditional premise. Find that conditional.
The missing conditional is: 'If a number is even, then it is not odd.'
2
See how the disjunctive syllogism reduces to a conditional one
The author states that the inference from 'it is even' to 'it is not odd' is only valid by presupposing a mental conditional syllogism. Write the conditional syllogism in standard form: Major premise: 'Every even is not odd'; Minor premise: 'This is even'; Conclusion: 'This is not odd'. This shows that the disjunctive argument depends on a conditional.
Similarly, the other case ('not even' to 'odd') presupposes 'Every non-even is odd'.
3
Understand the absolutist claim being critiqued
Some investigators claimed that in a conditional syllogism, if the antecedent is doubtful, its clarification necessarily requires a conjunctive syllogism to avoid infinite regress. The author rejects this as negligence. Articulate this claim in your own words.
The claim is that every exclusive syllogism ultimately relies on a conjunctive syllogism.
4
Follow the author's refutation
The author gives counterexamples: sometimes a thing is clear in itself (bayyin bi-nafsihi) and its implication is unclear only through that clarity. The antecedent may be clear without a conjunctive proof. Thus, the need for a conjunctive syllogism is not universal but only in most cases.
The author emphasizes that clarity does not imply that the consequent is clear; the implication may be known by proof.
5
Evaluate the conclusion
The author's chosen view is that the necessity of conjunctive syllogisms for exclusive ones is not absolute. Distinguish between cases where the antecedent is clear by itself and cases where it requires proof. Reflect on the epistemological nuance.
This is an important refinement in the logic of syllogisms.

Chapter Two on defining what was said that exclusive syllogisms are not completed by conjunctive ones, and verifying the statement therein. We say: The division of the exclusive into conditional and disjunctive has appeared. If the conditional is not completed without the conjunctive, then it is apparent that the disjunctive is not completed except by it, due to the necessity that the conditional is not completed without the conditional. For if you say in the case of the conjunctive-disjunctive exclusive: 'Every number is either even or odd,' then you say: 'But it is even,' even though it does not follow from it that it is not odd, it is only by presupposing a mental conditional syllogism, which is: 'Everything that is even is not odd.' So if it is said that it is even, it follows that it is not odd. Similarly, if it is said that it is not even, the necessity of being odd is also only by presupposing a mental conditional syllogism, which is: 'Everything that is not even is odd.' So if it is said that it is not even, it follows that it is odd. And likewise for the exclusive disjunction without the conjunctive, and the exclusive conjunction without the disjunctive. At this point we say: The conditional is either such that the exception is exactly the antecedent of the conditional clause, or the negation of its consequent. If it is the first, then what some investigators undertook to explain its need for the conjunctive is that they said: If the antecedent were clear, the consequent would be clear for it, and there would be no meaning to the syllogism, because it only clarifies what is hidden. Therefore, it must be unclear, and then its clarification would certainly not be without the conjunctive, due to infinite regress. But this is negligence in investigation, for sometimes a thing is clear in itself and its implication is unclear except through the mediation of its implication for that thing clear in itself. And the meaning of a thing being clear in itself is not that it is clear by means of its implication for the clear; otherwise all things would become clear in themselves, due to the necessity of their termination in clarity to what is clear in itself. Then how [could it be]? And sometimes a thing is clear and the implication of the consequent is not clear except by a proof. Then even if one concedes the necessity of the antecedent being doubtful, its clarification would only be required by a conjunctive syllogism, or by something less in the clarification to it. Rather, perhaps it is clarified by what is clear in itself without a syllogism, and its implication for that clear in clarification is like the implication of the consequent for the antecedent when the antecedent is clear in itself, or it is clear by an exclusive syllogism whose termination in clarification is like this clear. Then if what was said about the explanation of the need for the conjunctive is necessary, it is only in most cases, not that it is absolutely necessary. And if the exception is the negation of the consequent of the conditional, the verification of the consequent being doubtful and of its need for the conjunctive in clarification is according to what we have recognized of the refuted and the chosen.
Source pages p. 430

Commentary
This passage offers an analysis of the relationship between exclusive syllogisms (الاستثنائية) and conjunctive syllogisms (الاقترانية). The author first reiterates the division of exclusive syllogisms into conditional (متصل) and disjunctive (منفصل). He argues that disjunctive syllogisms, such as those of the form 'every number is either even or odd' (مانعة الجمع والخلو), require a mental conditional syllogism to complete the inference. For example, from 'it is even' to 'it is not odd', one presupposes the conditional 'every even is not odd'. Similarly, from 'not even' to 'odd' presupposes 'every non-even is odd'. This shows that even disjunctive syllogisms depend on an underlying conditional. The author then focuses on the case where the exception (المستثنى) in a conditional syllogism is exactly the antecedent of the conditional clause. He critiques a claim made by some investigators that the antecedent must be doubtful and that its clarification necessarily requires a conjunctive syllogism to avoid an infinite regress. The author contends that this is negligence (تغافل) because sometimes a thing is clear in itself (بين بنفسه) and its implication (لازم) is unclear only through that clarity. He provides the example where clarity does not imply that the consequent is clear; rather, the implication may be known only by a proof. He further argues that even if one assumes the antecedent is doubtful, its clarification does not always require a conjunctive syllogism; it might be clarified by something clearer, or even by itself without a syllogism. The need for a conjunctive syllogism is not absolute but only in most cases (في أكثر الأمر). Finally, he mentions that if the exception is the negation of the consequent, the situation is according to the refuted and chosen views earlier. The author's chosen view (المختار) is that the necessity is not universal.

المتصل
المنفصل

Key Takeaway
The need for conjunctive syllogisms in exclusive syllogisms is not absolute; some conditionals are clear by themselves and do not require further proof.

The Syllogism

Every Syllogism Requires Exactly Two Premises
Author argues that every syllogism requires exactly two premises, not more.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to justify why conjunctive and exceptive syllogisms each require exactly two premises and distinguish between essential and extraneous statements in a syllogistic argument.

Core Idea
Two premises are enough; the third is either redundant or irrelevant.

In this passage, the author establishes a fundamental constraint on syllogistic reasoning: whether conjunctive or exceptive, a valid syllogism requires exactly two premises. He argues based on the structure of the conclusion and the role of the middle term. Understanding this principle helps you evaluate whether an argument is properly formed or padded with unnecessary statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of syllogisms
Locate in the passage the terms 'qiyas iqtirani' (conjunctive) and 'qiyas istithna'i' (exceptive). Note that the author treats them separately to show the two-premise requirement holds for both.
These are the two main divisions of syllogism in Islamic logic.
2
Analyze the conjunctive syllogism argument
The author says the conclusion (matlub) has two parts: subject and predicate. Each part must have a relation to one premise. Since there are exactly two parts, exactly two premises are needed. A third premise would either duplicate an existing relation or relate to something outside the conclusion.
This is a structural argument based on the composition of the conclusion.
3
Analyze the exceptive syllogism argument
The exceptive syllogism requires a conditional premise (either muttasil or munfasil) and a second premise that affirms or negates one part. Earlier proofs showed these two premises suffice, so no third is needed.
The second premise is the 'istithna' (exception) that triggers the conclusion.
4
Examine the treatment of additional statements
The author acknowledges that additional statements may accompany a syllogism, but they are either clarifications (for the premises), embellishments, or independent discourse. They are not part of the core syllogism.
This helps you distinguish the logical argument from rhetorical additions.

الفن الثالث في القياسات المركبة وإشباع القول فيها فنقول: قد بان في ما سلف انقسام القياس إلى الاقتراني والاستثنائي، وأن كل واحد منهما لا يتم من أقل من مقدمتين، فكذا لا يكون من أكثر منهما، والمطلوب واحد. أما إذا كان القياس اقترانياً ولا تبالي من أي شكل كان ومن أي اقتران منه، فلأن المطلوب أكثر ما يكون تركبه من جزأين مبتدأ وخبر، وعند ذلك فلا بد وأن يكون لكل واحد منهما نسبة إلى مقدمة من مقدمات قياس المطلوب – أي يكون فيها مبتدأ أو خبراً - ولا أكثر من جزأين فلا أكثر من مقدمتين، فإن الثالثة إن ناسبت ما ناسبته غيرها فلا حاجة إليها، وإن ناسبت شيئاً خارجاً عن أجزاء المطلوب فلا حاجة إليها أيضاً، فإنه مهما كان بين المقدمتين المناسبتين لجزأي المطلوب حد مشترك كما سلف وكانت نسبته إليهما - أي جزأي المطلوب - من إحدى النسب الأربع السالف ذكرها، قد اقترنت بالشرائط المشار إليها فالمطلوب لازم لا محالة، والاستثناء واقع عن مقدمة ثالثة خارجة عن المقدمتين المناسبتين. وأما إن كان استثنائياً فلأنه لا بُدّ فيه من مقدمة شرطية إما متصلة أو منفصلة، وقد بينا أن الإنتاج يتم بمقدمة ثانية استثنائية بعين أحد الجزأين، أو نقيضه على الشروط الممهدة من قبل، وإذ ذاك فلا حاجة إلى مقدمة ثالثة تكون هي الاستثنائية أو المستثنى منها. نعم، إن أدرج مع القياس للمطلوب قول آخر زائد على المقدمتين فهو إما لبيان المقدمتين، أو إحداهما، أو لمعنى من خارج، والذي هو لمعنى من خارج فمنه ما لا يتعلق بالقياس ولا بأحد أجزائه، فكلام مستقل بنفسه وقع مقارناً للقياس لا يعجز الناظر عن تمييزه عنه. ومنه ما له نوع تعلق بأجزاء القياس، فمنه ما يكون للتحسين والتزيين وترويج
The third art: on compound syllogisms and expanding the discourse on them. We say: It has been clarified in what preceded the division of syllogism into conjunctive and exceptive, and that each one of them is not completed with less than two premises, so likewise it is not with more than them, and the conclusion is one. As for if the syllogism is conjunctive, regardless of which figure it is and which conjunction it is from, it is because the conclusion is most often composed of two parts: subject and predicate. And accordingly, each one of them must have a relation to a premise from the premises of the syllogism of the conclusion – i.e., it is in it either as subject or predicate – and there are no more than two parts, so there are no more than two premises. For the third, if it relates to what the other relates to, there is no need for it; and if it relates to something outside the parts of the conclusion, there is also no need for it. For whenever there is a common term between the two premises appropriate for the two parts of the conclusion, as previously, and its relation to them – i.e., the two parts of the conclusion – is one of the four relations previously mentioned, and it is conjoined with the conditions indicated, then the conclusion follows necessarily, and the exception is made regarding a third premise outside the two appropriate premises. And as for if it is exceptive, it is because it must involve a conditional premise, either conjunctive or disjunctive, and we have shown that the production is completed with a second exceptive premise specifying one of the two parts or its negation, according to the conditions prepared earlier. And in that case, there is no need for a third premise that would be the exceptive or the exception from it. Yes, if another statement beyond the two premises is included with the syllogism for the conclusion, it is either for clarifying the two premises or one of them, or for an external meaning. As for what is for an external meaning, some of it has no connection to the syllogism or any of its parts, so it is an independent discourse that occurs accompanying the syllogism, and the observer is not incapable of distinguishing it from the syllogism. And some of it has a kind of connection to the parts of the syllogism, and some of it is for embellishment, adornment, and promotion.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage is from the third section on compound syllogisms. The author states that it has been established that syllogisms are divided into conjunctive (iqtirani) and exceptive (istithna'i), and each requires at least two premises, but not more, because the conclusion (matlub) is one. For the conjunctive syllogism, regardless of its figure or mood, the conclusion is composed of a subject and a predicate. Each of these two parts must be related to one of the premises, so exactly two premises are needed. A third premise is either redundant (if it relates to what another premise already relates to) or irrelevant (if it relates to something outside the parts of the conclusion). As long as the two premises have a common term (middle term) that relates to the two parts of the conclusion in one of the four valid relations (subject-subject, subject-predicate, etc.) with the proper conditions, the conclusion follows necessarily. For the exceptive syllogism, it requires a conditional premise (either conjunctive 'if-then' or disjunctive 'either-or') and a second premise that affirms or negates one of the two parts (antecedent/consequent or one disjunct), as shown earlier. A third premise is not needed. Any additional statement included with the syllogism is either for clarifying the premises, for an external purpose, or for rhetorical embellishment. An example from Islamic law: in a conjunctive syllogism, 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; khamr is an intoxicant, therefore khamr is forbidden' uses exactly two premises. An exceptive syllogism: 'If it is wine, then it is forbidden; it is wine, therefore it is forbidden' again uses two premises. The author emphasizes that a third premise is either unnecessary or not part of the core syllogistic reasoning.

Key Terms
القياس الاقتراني
القياس الاستثنائي
المطلوب
المقدمة

Checkpoint
According to the text, why does a conjunctive syllogism require exactly two premises and not more?
A) Because the conclusion has two parts (subject and predicate), so only two premises are needed.
B) Because the syllogism must have a middle term.
C) Because three premises would make the argument invalid.
D) Because the first figure requires two premises.

Key Takeaway
Both conjunctive and exceptive syllogisms are necessarily composed of exactly two premises; any additional statement is either clarifying, external, or rhetorical, not part of the core logical proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Take the following exceptive syllogism: 'If a woman menstruates, she does not pray. Zaynab is menstruating. Therefore, she does not pray.' Identify the two premises and the conclusion. Then add a third premise: 'Menstruation is a natural condition.' Explain why this third premise is unnecessary for the validity of the syllogism.
?
2. Find a syllogism in a fiqh manual (e.g., Muwatta' of Imam Malik or a usul text) and confirm that the core argument uses exactly two premises. If it includes additional statements, classify them as clarification, embellishment, or external.

The Syllogism
intermediate
7 min
Why a Syllogism Has Exactly Two Premises
Author argues the two-premise limit for all syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to justify why a categorical or hypothetical syllogism cannot have more than two premises using the subject-predicate structure of the conclusion.

Core Idea
A syllogism needs two premises because the conclusion has only two parts: subject and predicate.

The author begins by reaffirming a basic but crucial rule: every syllogism, whether conjunctive or hypothetical, requires exactly two premises — no more, no less. He explains why this is so by analyzing the structure of the desired conclusion, which always consists of a subject and a predicate. A third premise would either duplicate an already covered relation or be completely irrelevant to the conclusion's parts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parts of any conclusion
The conclusion (māṭlūb) always has a subject (mubtadaʾ) and a predicate (khabar). In the author's words: 'المطلوب أكثر ما يكون تركيبه من جزأين مبتدأ وخبر'.
For example, in 'Every B is J', B is the subject and J is the predicate.
2
Relate each part to a premise
Each of these two parts must appear in exactly one of the two premises. The author states that each part must have a relation (munāsaba) to a premise, i.e., it must be the subject or predicate of that premise.
The middle term (ḥadd mushtarak) connects the two premises.
3
See why a third premise is unnecessary
If the third premise relates to something that the first two already cover, it is redundant. If it relates to something outside the conclusion's parts, it is irrelevant. The author says: 'فإن الثالثة إن ناسبت ما ناسبته غيرها فلا حاجة إليها، وإن ناسبت شيئاً خارجاً عن أجزاء المطلوب فلا حاجة إليها أيضاً'.
This argument applies to both conjunctive and hypothetical syllogisms.

فمنه ما يكون للتحسين والتزيين وترويج الكلام، كما يقال: زيد الطويل الضخم شجاع وكل شجاع (مقدام) فـ (زيد الطويل الضخم مقدام) وليس دخول الطويل والضخم في الكلام على أنه من أجزاء القياس بل لترويج قولنا: أن إما للحذر للتنبيه على كذبهما عند التصريح بها، أو للاكتفاء بشهرتها عن التصريح بها أول ظهورها عند انتظام مقدمتي قياسها، كما في المقدمة الصغرى في قياس المطلوب السابق، كما ذكره بالنسبة إلى مقدمتيها، فعلى The third art: On compound syllogisms and exhausting the discussion about them. We say: It has become clear in what preceded the division of syllogism into conjunctive and hypothetical, and that each one of them is not completed from less than two premises, so likewise it is not from more than them, and the desired [conclusion] is one. As for if the syllogism is conjunctive, and it does not matter from which figure it is and from which conjunction of it, because the desired [conclusion] most of the time is composed of two parts, subject and predicate, and at that time it is necessary that each one of them have a relation to a premise from the premises of the syllogism of the desired [conclusion] – i.e., it is in it as subject or predicate – and not more than two parts, so not more than two premises. For the third, if it relates to what another relates to, then there is no need for it; and if it relates to something outside the parts of the desired [conclusion], then there is also no need for it. For whenever there is between the two premises appropriate to the two parts of the desired [conclusion] a common term as previously, and its relation to them – i.e., the two parts of the desired [conclusion] – is one of the four relations previously mentioned, and they have been combined with the conditions indicated, then the desired [conclusion] is necessary inevitably, and exception occurs from a third premise outside the two appropriate premises. And as for if it is hypothetical, because it necessarily requires a conditional premise, either conjunctive or disjunctive, and we have shown that production is completed by a second hypothetical premise [that states] the same as one of the two parts or its contradictory, according to the conditions prepared before, and at that time there is no need for a third premise that would be the hypothetical or the excepted from it. Yes, if another statement beyond the two premises is included with the syllogism for the desired [conclusion], then it is either for clarifying the two premises, or one of them, or for an external meaning. And that which is for an external meaning includes what is not related to the syllogism nor to any of its parts, so it is an independent speech that happens to be accompanying the syllogism, and the observer is not incapable of distinguishing it from it. And it includes what has some relation to the parts of the syllogism, and from that is for embellishment and beautification and promoting the speech, as it is said: Zayd the tall, the big is brave, and every brave is (daring); so Zayd the tall, the big is daring. And the entry of 'tall' and 'big' in the speech is not as parts of the syllogism, but for promoting our statement that Zayd is brave. And from it is for clarification and making the speech apparent, like giving examples and citations and other than that. And as for that which is for clarifying the two premises or one of them, then either it is of the syllogistic pattern or not of the syllogistic pattern. And on both estimates, the syllogism that clarifies the desired [conclusion] together with the addition of the syllogism that clarifies its premise or its two premises is called a compound syllogism. It is not a single unified syllogism clarifying one desired [conclusion], but rather multiple syllogisms that preceded to clarify one desired [conclusion], and the syllogism that clarifies the desired [conclusion] from them essentially is only one. And that either occurs in conjunctives or hypotheticals. If it is in conjunctives, then the syllogism added to the desired syllogism is either clarifying the two premises of the desired syllogism or one of them. If it is clarifying one of them, then it is either the minor or the major. If it is the minor, then it is that you conclude by a syllogism of the premise and then proceed from it to the desired syllogism, even if far. Its example: if the desired [conclusion] is for example 'every B is J' and its two premises are 'every B is A' and 'every A is J', and that which is clarified from them by syllogism is for example 'every B is A', so we say: 'every B is D' and 'every D is A', so 'every B is A' and 'every A is J', so 'every B is J'. So the conclusions are pairs and the premises of each one of them are pairs. And if they are singular, it is only due to an increase of its premise in the aforementioned manner, or due to a decrease of a premise, either out of caution to warn of their falsehood when explicitly stating them, or out of being satisfied with their well-knownness over stating them, or their appearance when arranging the two premises of its syllogism, as in the minor premise in the desired syllogism previously, as he mentioned regarding its two premises. Thus, based on this, that in which the conclusions are explicitly stated is called a connected syllogism, and that in which the conclusions are omitted is called a disconnected syllogism. As for the example of the first, it is as we have stated. As for the example of the second, it is as in our saying in the previous example: (every B is D and every D is A and every A is J), so (every) so (every B is J). So the premises of the first are four, and the second three, and the terms from which the composition [is made] in both are four, since the terms of the first desired syllogism are three as known, given that one of its two premises is a result of another syllogism.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage is an extended discussion on compound syllogisms (القياسات المركبة). The author begins by reiterating a previously established point: both conjunctive (اقتراني) and hypothetical (استثنائي) syllogisms require exactly two premises, and no more, because the desired conclusion has only two parts (subject and predicate). A third premise would either duplicate one of the two needed relations or be irrelevant. Then the author considers cases where an additional statement accompanies the syllogism. This additional statement may serve to clarify the premises, or it may be for external purposes like embellishment, example-giving, or independent discourse. The focus here is on additions that prove the premises themselves. When a syllogism is used to prove one or both premises of the main syllogism, the entire structure is called a 'compound syllogism' (قياس مركب). It is not a single syllogism but a chain of syllogisms all aiming at one final conclusion. The author then gives a concrete example using logical variables: if the desired conclusion is 'every B is J', with premises 'every B is A' and 'every A is J', and if the premise 'every B is A' is itself proven by a sub-syllogism 'every B is D' and 'every D is A', then the composite argument is a compound syllogism. The author further distinguishes between two types: 'connected' (موصول) where intermediate conclusions are explicitly stated, and 'disconnected' (مفصول) where intermediate conclusions are omitted. Examples illustrate both: in the connected form, the full chain is written out with each step's result; in the disconnected form, the intermediate result is skipped, resulting in fewer explicit statements but the same terms. The passage thus provides a thorough explanation of how compound syllogisms are formed and their variations.

القياس الاستثنائي
المقدمة
المطلوب
المبتدأ
الخبر
الحد المشترك

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does a conjunctive syllogism require exactly two premises?
A) Because the conclusion has a subject and predicate, and each part must be linked by a premise.
B) Because the conclusion is always a conditional statement.
C) Because the syllogism must have a middle term, and two premises provide exactly one middle term.
D) Because the syllogism must be in the first figure.

Key Takeaway
Every syllogism needs exactly two premises because its conclusion has only two logical parts, and a third premise is always unnecessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Syllogism

What Is a Compound Syllogism?
Author defines compound syllogism and distinguishes it from other additions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define a compound syllogism and identify whether an extended argument qualifies as one.

Core Idea
A compound syllogism is a chain of syllogisms where each premise of the main syllogism may itself be proven by another syllogism.

After establishing that a single syllogism uses only two premises, the author turns to cases where an additional statement is included. He categorizes such additions: some are for clarification (of premises), some for embellishment, and some for independent commentary. The focus is on the clarificatory type, which, when it takes a syllogistic form, results in a 'compound syllogism' (al-qiyās al-murakkab). This is not a single syllogism but multiple syllogisms working together to prove one final conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish additions that clarify premises
The author says anything beyond the two premises is either for clarifying the premises, for external meaning, or for embellishment. Identify which category the extra statement falls into.
Clarifying premises is the only type that can create a compound syllogism.
2
Check if the clarification is syllogistic
The clarifying addition may or may not be on the syllogistic pattern (ʿalā al-namṭ al-qiyāsī). Only when it is a syllogism itself does it produce a compound syllogism.
3
Define compound syllogism
The author states: 'القياس المبين للمطلوب مع ضميمة القياس المبين لمقدمته أو مقدمتيه يسمى قياساً مركباً'. It is a main syllogism plus an additional syllogism proving one or both of its premises.
It is not a single syllogism; it is a sequence aiming at one final conclusion.

Source Text

الفن الثالث
في القياسات المركبة وإشباع القول فيها
فنقول: قد بان في ما سلف انقسام القياس إلى الاقتراني والاستثنائي، وأن كل واحد منهما لا يتم من أقل من مقدمتين، فكذا لا يكون من أكثر منهما، والمطلوب واحد.
أما إذا كان القياس اقترانياً ولا تبالي من أي شكل كان ومن أي اقتران منه، فلأن المطلوب أكثر ما يكون تركيبه من جزأين مبتدأ وخبر، وعند ذلك فلا بد وأن يكون لكل واحد منهما نسبة إلى مقدمة من مقدمات قياس المطلوب – أي يكون فيها مبتدأ أو خبراً - ولا أكثر من جزأين فلا أكثر من مقدمتين، فإن الثالثة إن ناسبت ما ناسبته غيرها فلا حاجة إليها، وإن ناسبت شيئاً خارجاً عن أجزاء المطلوب فلا حاجة إليها أيضاً، فإنه مهما كان بين المقدمتين المناسبتين لجزأي المطلوب حد مشترك كما سلف وكانت نسبته إليهما - أي جزأي المطلوب - من إحدى النسب الأربع السالف ذكرها، قد اقترنت بالشرائط المشار إليها فالمطلوب لازم لا محالة، والاستثناء واقع عن مقدمة ثالثة خارجة عن المقدمتين المناسبتين. وأما إن كان استثنائياً فلأنه لا بُدّ فيه من مقدمة شرطية إما متصلة أو منفصلة، وقد بينا أن الإنتاج يتم بمقدمة ثانية استثنائية بعين أحد الجزأين، أو نقيضه على الشروط الممهدة من قبل، وإذ ذاك فلا حاجة إلى مقدمة ثالثة تكون هي الاستثنائية أو المستثنى منها. نعم، إن أدرج مع القياس للمطلوب قول آخر زائد على المقدمتين فهو إما لبيان المقدمتين، أو إحداهما، أو لمعنى من خارج، والذي هو لمعنى من خارج فمنه ما لا يتعلق بالقياس ولا بأحد أجزائه، فكلام مستقل بنفسه وقع مقارناً للقياس لا يعجز الناظر عن تمييزه عنه. ومنه ما له نوع تعلق بأجزاء القياس، فمنه ما يكون للتحسين والتزيين وترويج الكلام، كما يقال: زيد الطويل الضخم شجاع وكل شجاع (مقدام) فـ (زيد الطويل الضخم مقدام) وليس دخول الطويل والضخم في الكلام على أنه من أجزاء القياس بل لترويج قولنا: أن زيداً شجاع. ومنه يكون للإيضاح وإظهار الكلام كضرب الأمثلة والاستشهادات وغير ذلك. وأما ما هو لبيان المقدمتين أو إحداهما فإما أن يكون النمط القياسي أو ليس على النمط القياسي، وعلى كلا التقديرين فالقياس المبين للمطلوب مع ضميمة القياس المبين لمقدمته أو مقدمتيه يسمى قياساً مركباً) لا أنه قياس متحد مبين المطلوب واحد، بل أقيسة سبقت لبيان مطلوب واحد والقياس المبين للمطلوب منها بالذات ليس إلا واحداً، وذلك إما أن يقع في الاقترانيات أو الاستثنائيات، فإن كان في الاقترانيات فالقياس المضاف إلى القياس المطلوب إما أن يكون مبيناً المقدمتي قياس المطلوب أو لإحداهما ، فإن كان مبيناً لإحداهما فإما الصغرى أو الكبري، فإن كانت الصغرى فهو أن تقضي بقياس المقدمة ثم تدرج منه إلى قياس المطلوب وإن بعد – ونظيره إذا كان المطلوب مثلاً كل "ب" "ج") ومقدمتاه (كل "ب" "أ") و(كل "أ" "ج") والمبين منهما بالقياس مثلاً كل "ب" "أ") فنقول (كل "ب" "د" وكل "د" "أ") فـ (كل "ب" "أ") و(كل "أ" "ج") فـ (كل "ب" "ج") فالنتائج زوج ومقدمات كل واحدة منهما زوج، وإن كانت مفردة فليس إلا لزيادة مقدمته على النحو المذكور سلف، أو لنقصان مقدمة إما للحذر للتنبيه على كذبهما عند التصريح بها، أو للاكتفاء بشهرتها عن التصريح بها أول ظهورها عند انتظام مقدمتي قياسها، كما في المقدمة الصغرى في قياس المطلوب السابق، كما ذكره بالنسبة إلى مقدمتيها، فعلى هذا ما صرح فيه بالنتائج فيسمى قياساً موصولاً) وما أهمل فيه النتائج فيسمى قياساً مفصولاً
أما مثال الأول فكما أسلفناه، وأما مثال الثاني فكما في قولنا في المثال السابق ((كل) "ب" "د" وكل "د" "أ" وكل "أ" "ج") فـ (كل ) فـ (كل "ب" "ج") فتكون مقدمات الأول أربعة، والثاني ثلاثة والحدود التي منها التأليف فيهما أربعة، إذ الحدود لقياس المطلوب الأول ثلاثة على ما عرف فإذا كانت إحدى مقدمتيه نتيجة لقياس آخر
The third art: On compound syllogisms and exhausting the discussion about them. We say: It has become clear in what preceded the division of syllogism into conjunctive and hypothetical, and that each one of them is not completed from less than two premises, so likewise it is not from more than them, and the desired [conclusion] is one. As for if the syllogism is conjunctive, and it does not matter from which figure it is and from which conjunction of it, because the desired [conclusion] most of the time is composed of two parts, subject and predicate, and at that time it is necessary that each one of them have a relation to a premise from the premises of the syllogism of the desired [conclusion] – i.e., it is in it as subject or predicate – and not more than two parts, so not more than two premises. For the third, if it relates to what another relates to, then there is no need for it; and if it relates to something outside the parts of the desired [conclusion], then there is also no need for it. For whenever there is between the two premises appropriate to the two parts of the desired [conclusion] a common term as previously, and its relation to them – i.e., the two parts of the desired [conclusion] – is one of the four relations previously mentioned, and they have been combined with the conditions indicated, then the desired [conclusion] is necessary inevitably, and exception occurs from a third premise outside the two appropriate premises. And as for if it is hypothetical, because it necessarily requires a conditional premise, either conjunctive or disjunctive, and we have shown that production is completed by a second hypothetical premise [that states] the same as one of the two parts or its contradictory, according to the conditions prepared before, and at that time there is no need for a third premise that would be the hypothetical or the excepted from it. Yes, if another statement beyond the two premises is included with the syllogism for the desired [conclusion], then it is either for clarifying the two premises, or one of them, or for an external meaning. And that which is for an external meaning includes what is not related to the syllogism nor to any of its parts, so it is an independent speech that happens to be accompanying the syllogism, and the observer is not incapable of distinguishing it from it. And it includes what has some relation to the parts of the syllogism, and from that is for embellishment and beautification and promoting the speech, as it is said: Zayd the tall, the big is brave, and every brave is (daring); so Zayd the tall, the big is daring. And the entry of 'tall' and 'big' in the speech is not as parts of the syllogism, but for promoting our statement that Zayd is brave. And from it is for clarification and making the speech apparent, like giving examples and citations and other than that. And as for that which is for clarifying the two premises or one of them, then either it is of the syllogistic pattern or not of the syllogistic pattern. And on both estimates, the syllogism that clarifies the desired [conclusion] together with the addition of the syllogism that clarifies its premise or its two premises is called a compound syllogism. It is not a single unified syllogism clarifying one desired [conclusion], but rather multiple syllogisms that preceded to clarify one desired [conclusion], and the syllogism that clarifies the desired [conclusion] from them essentially is only one. And that either occurs in conjunctives or hypotheticals. If it is in conjunctives, then the syllogism added to the desired syllogism is either clarifying the two premises of the desired syllogism or one of them. If it is clarifying one of them, then it is either the minor or the major. If it is the minor, then it is that you conclude by a syllogism of the premise and then proceed from it to the desired syllogism, even if far. Its example: if the desired [conclusion] is for example 'every B is J' and its two premises are 'every B is A' and 'every A is J', and that which is clarified from them by syllogism is for example 'every B is A', so we say: 'every B is D' and 'every D is A', so 'every B is A' and 'every A is J', so 'every B is J'. So the conclusions are pairs and the premises of each one of them are pairs. And if they are singular, it is only due to an increase of its premise in the aforementioned manner, or due to a decrease of a premise, either out of caution to warn of their falsehood when explicitly stating them, or out of being satisfied with their well-knownness over stating them, or their appearance when arranging the two premises of its syllogism, as in the minor premise in the desired syllogism previously, as he mentioned regarding its two premises. Thus, based on this, that in which the conclusions are explicitly stated is called a connected syllogism, and that in which the conclusions are omitted is called a disconnected syllogism. As for the example of the first, it is as we have stated. As for the example of the second, it is as in our saying in the previous example: (every B is D and every D is A and every A is J), so (every) so (every B is J). So the premises of the first are four, and the second three, and the terms from which the composition [is made] in both are four, since the terms of the first desired syllogism are three as known, given that one of its two premises is a result of another syllogism.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage is an extended discussion on compound syllogisms (القياسات المركبة). The author begins by reiterating a previously established point: both conjunctive (اقتراني) and hypothetical (استثنائي) syllogisms require exactly two premises, and no more, because the desired conclusion has only two parts (subject and predicate). A third premise would either duplicate one of the two needed relations or be irrelevant. Then the author considers cases where an additional statement accompanies the syllogism. This additional statement may serve to clarify the premises, or it may be for external purposes like embellishment, example-giving, or independent discourse. The focus here is on additions that prove the premises themselves. When a syllogism is used to prove one or both premises of the main syllogism, the entire structure is called a 'compound syllogism' (قياس مركب). It is not a single syllogism but a chain of syllogisms all aiming at one final conclusion. The author then gives a concrete example using logical variables: if the desired conclusion is 'every B is J', with premises 'every B is A' and 'every A is J', and if the premise 'every B is A' is itself proven by a sub-syllogism 'every B is D' and 'every D is A', then the composite argument is a compound syllogism. The author further distinguishes between two types: 'connected' (موصول) where intermediate conclusions are explicitly stated, and 'disconnected' (مفصول) where intermediate conclusions are omitted. Examples illustrate both: in the connected form, the full chain is written out with each step's result; in the disconnected form, the intermediate result is skipped, resulting in fewer explicit statements but the same terms. The passage thus provides a thorough explanation of how compound syllogisms are formed and their variations.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does an additional statement beyond the two premises result in a compound syllogism?
A) When it is for embellishment and beautification.
B) When it is an independent speech that accompanies the syllogism.
C) When it clarifies a premise and is itself of the syllogistic pattern.
D) When it is an example or citation.

Key Takeaway
A compound syllogism is a chain of syllogisms where the main syllogism's premises are proven by additional syllogisms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the following extended argument in usul al-fiqh: 'Every alcoholic beverage is khamr. Every khamr is forbidden. But is nabidh khamr? We can prove: Every intoxicating drink is khamr. Nabidh is intoxicating. Therefore nabidh is khamr. Thus, nabidh is forbidden.' Identify the main syllogism and the sub-syllogism. Is this a compound syllogism? Explain.
?
Construct your own compound syllogism on an Islamic topic (e.g., obligation of prayer, prohibition of riba) by first proving one of the premises with a sub-syllogism.

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
Connected vs. Disconnected Compound Syllogisms
Author distinguishes connected and disconnected compound syllogisms with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a connected (mawṣūl) and disconnected (mafṣūl) compound syllogism and provide an example of each.

Core Idea
A connected syllogism states intermediate conclusions explicitly; a disconnected one omits them, listing only the premises of sub-syllogisms.

The author now elaborates on two sub-types of compound syllogisms, particularly in the conjunctive form. He explains the structure using the example of proving the minor premise of a main syllogism. The key distinction is whether the intermediate conclusions (results of sub-syllogisms) are explicitly stated (connected) or omitted (disconnected). The passage provides concrete variable-based examples.

Guided Path
1
Set up the main syllogism and its proof
The main syllogism has conclusion 'كل ب ج' with premises 'كل ب أ' and 'كل أ ج'. Suppose we need to prove the minor premise 'كل ب أ' with a sub-syllogism: 'كل ب د' and 'كل د أ'.
This is a compound syllogism because one premise is proven by another syllogism.
2
Define the connected form (موصول)
In the connected form, we explicitly state the intermediate conclusion 'كل ب أ' after proving it. The full argument is: (كل ب د وكل د أ → كل ب أ) ثم (كل ب أ وكل أ ج → كل ب ج). The intermediate result is stated.
The author says: 'ما صرح فيه بالنتائج فيسمى قياساً موصولاً'.
3
Define the disconnected form (مفصول)
In the disconnected form, we omit the intermediate result and just list all premises: كل ب د، كل د أ، كل أ ج، then directly conclude كل ب ج. The intermediate 'كل ب أ' is not stated.
The author says: 'ما أهمل فيه النتائج فيسمى قياساً مفصولاً'.
4
Compare the number of premises and terms
In the connected example, premises are four (two for sub-syllogism, two for main). In the disconnected, premises are three (since the intermediate result is omitted). The terms are four in both: (ب, د, أ, ج) because each syllogism introduces one new term (د) beyond the main syllogism's three (ب, أ, ج).

The third art: On compound syllogisms and exhausting the discussion about them. We say: It has become clear in what preceded the division of syllogism into conjunctive and hypothetical, and that each one of them is not completed from less than two premises, so likewise it is not from more than them, and the desired [conclusion] is one. As for if the syllogism is conjunctive, and it does not matter from which figure it is and from which conjunction of it, because the desired [conclusion] most of the time is composed of two parts, subject and predicate, and at that time it is necessary that each one of them have a relation to a premise from the premises of the syllogism of the desired [conclusion] – i.e., it is in it as subject or predicate – and not more than two parts, so not more than two premises. For the third, if it relates to what another relates to, then there is no need for it; and if it relates to something outside the parts of the desired [conclusion], then there is also no need for it. For whenever there is between the two premises appropriate to the two parts of the desired [conclusion] a common term as previously, and its relation to them – i.e., the two parts of the desired [conclusion] – is one of the four relations previously mentioned, and they have been combined with the conditions indicated, then the desired [conclusion] is necessary inevitably, and exception occurs from a third premise outside the two appropriate premises. And as for if it is hypothetical, because it necessarily requires a conditional premise, either conjunctive or disjunctive, and we have shown that production is completed by a second hypothetical premise [that states] the same as one of the two parts or its contradictory, according to the conditions prepared before, and at that time there is no need for a third premise that would be the hypothetical or the excepted from it. Yes, if another statement beyond the two premises is included with the syllogism for the desired [conclusion], then it is either for clarifying the two premises, or one of them, or for an external meaning. And that which is for an external meaning includes what is not related to the syllogism nor to any of its parts, so it is an independent speech that happens to be accompanying the syllogism, and the observer is not incapable of distinguishing it from it. And it includes what has some relation to the parts of the syllogism, and from that is for embellishment and beautification and promoting the speech, as it is said: Zayd the tall, the big is brave, and every brave is (daring); so Zayd the tall, the big is daring. And the entry of 'tall' and 'big' in the speech is not as parts of the syllogism, but for promoting our statement that Zayd is brave. And from it is for clarification and making the speech apparent, like giving examples and citations and other than that. And as for that which is for clarifying the two premises or one of them, then either it is of the syllogistic pattern or not of the syllogistic pattern. And on both estimates, the syllogism that clarifies the desired [conclusion] together with the addition of the syllogism that clarifies its premise or its two premises is called a compound syllogism. It is not a single unified syllogism clarifying one desired [conclusion], but rather multiple syllogisms that preceded to clarify one desired [conclusion], and the syllogism that clarifies the desired [conclusion] from them essentially is only one. And that either occurs in conjunctives or hypotheticals. If it is in conjunctives, then the syllogism added to the desired syllogism is either clarifying the two premises of the desired syllogism or one of them. If it is clarifying one of them, then it is either the minor or the major. If it is the minor, then it is that you conclude by a syllogism of the premise and then proceed from it to the desired syllogism, even if far. Its example: if the desired [conclusion] is for example 'every B is J' and its two premises are 'every B is A' and 'every A is J', and that which is clarified from them by syllogism is for example 'every B is A', so we say: 'every B is D' and 'every D is A', so 'every B is A' and 'every A is J', so 'every B is J'. So the conclusions are pairs and the premises of each one of them are pairs. And if they are singular, it is only due to an increase of its premise in the aforementioned manner, or due to a decrease of a premise, either out of caution to warn of their falsehood when explicitly stating them, or out of being satisfied with their well-knownness over stating them, or their appearance when arranging the two premises of its syllogism, as in the minor premise in the desired syllogism previously, as he mentioned regarding its two premises. Thus, based on this, that in which the conclusions are explicitly stated is called a connected syllogism, and that in which the conclusions are omitted is called a disconnected syllogism. As for the example of the first, it is as we have stated. As for the example of the second, it is as in our saying in the previous example: (every B is D and every D is A and every A is J), so (every) so (every B is J). So the premises of the first are four, and the second three, and the terms from which the composition [is made] in both are four, since the terms of the first desired syllogism are three as known, given that one of its two premises is a result of another syllogism.
Source pages p. 432

Commentary
This passage is an extended discussion on compound syllogisms (القياسات المركبة). The author begins by reiterating a previously established point: both conjunctive (اقتراني) and hypothetical (استثنائي) syllogisms require exactly two premises, and no more, because the desired conclusion has only two parts (subject and predicate). A third premise would either duplicate one of the two needed relations or be irrelevant. Then the author considers cases where an additional statement accompanies the syllogism. This additional statement may serve to clarify the premises, or it may be for external purposes like embellishment, example-giving, or independent discourse. The focus here is on additions that prove the premises themselves. When a syllogism is used to prove one or both premises of the main syllogism, the entire structure is called a 'compound syllogism' (قياس مركب). It is not a single syllogism but a chain of syllogisms all aiming at one final conclusion. The author then gives a concrete example using logical variables: if the desired conclusion is 'every B is J', with premises 'every B is A' and 'every A is J', and if the premise 'every B is A' is itself proven by a sub-syllogism 'every B is D' and 'every D is A', then the composite argument is a compound syllogism. The author further distinguishes between two types: 'connected' (موصول) where intermediate conclusions are explicitly stated, and 'disconnected' (مفصول) where intermediate conclusions are omitted. Examples illustrate both: in the connected form, the full chain is written out with each step's result; in the disconnected form, the intermediate result is skipped, resulting in fewer explicit statements but the same terms. The passage thus provides a thorough explanation of how compound syllogisms are formed and their variations.

القياس المفصول

Key Takeaway
Compound syllogisms can be presented with explicit intermediate conclusions (connected) or with them omitted (disconnected), but both are logically equivalent.

?

?

The Syllogism

Compound Syllogism: Connected vs. Separated
Author defines compound syllogism and distinguishes connected from separated types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a muttasil and a mafusul compound syllogism and identify which type is used in a given multi-step argument.

Core Idea
A compound syllogism is a chain of syllogisms; its connectedness depends on whether intermediate conclusions are explicit.

This passage defines the compound syllogism, which is not a single syllogism but a series of syllogisms working together to prove one conclusion. The author then introduces a crucial distinction: whether all intermediate conclusions are explicitly stated (muttasil) or some are omitted (mafusul). Understanding this distinction helps you analyze complex arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition
Find the definition of compound syllogism: it is a syllogism combined with another syllogism that proves one of its premises. It is not a single unified syllogism but multiple syllogisms leading to one conclusion.
The passage states that the syllogism clarifying the desired conclusion along with the syllogism clarifying its premise(s) is called a compound syllogism.
2
Understand the role of intermediate conclusions
In a compound syllogism, each proving syllogism produces an intermediate conclusion that serves as a premise for the next syllogism. The final conclusion is the desired one. The passage mentions that intermediate conclusions may be even in number or odd depending on added premises.
The example with B, A, C, D shows two syllogisms: one to prove the minor premise, then the main syllogism.
3
Distinguish muttasil and mafusul
The text explicitly states: 'ما صرح فيه بالنتائج فيسمى قياساً موصولاً وما أهمل فيه النتائج فيسمى قياساً مفصولاً'. So muttasil: all intermediate conclusions are explicit; mafusul: some are omitted.
Omission may occur for caution (if stating a false premise would be detrimental), for brevity (well-known premise), or because the conclusion becomes obvious when premises are arranged.
4
Apply to an example
Trace the passage's example: to prove 'every B is C', we need 'every B is A' and 'every A is C'. If 'every B is A' itself needs proof, we add a syllogism with D. If we explicitly write 'every B is A' after proving it, the argument is muttasil. If we omit that intermediate conclusion and go directly from the premises to the final conclusion, it is mafusul.
Practice with Islamic examples: proving that the world has a cause often involves a compound syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام، كما يقال: زيد الطويل الضخم شجاع وكل شجاع مقدام فزيد الطويل الضخم مقدام وليس دخول الطويل والضخم في الكلام على أنه من أجزاء القياس بل لترويج قولنا: أن زيداً شجاع ومنه يكون للإيضاح وإظهار الكلام كضرب الأمثلة والاستشهادات وغير ذلك. وأما ما هو لبيان المقدمتين أو إحداهما فإما أن يكون النمط القياسي أو ليس على النمط القياسي، وعلى كلا التقديرين فالقياس المبين للمطلوب مع ضميمة القياس المبين لمقدمته أو مقدمتيه يسمى قياساً مركباً لا أنه قياس متحد مبين المطلوب واحد، بل أقيسة سبقت لبيان مطلوب واحد والقياس المبين للمطلوب منها بالذات ليس إلا واحداً، وذلك إما أن يقع في الاقترانيات أو الاستثنائيات، فإن كان في الاقترانيات فالقياس المضاف إلى القياس المطلوب إما أن يكون مبيناً المقدمتي قياس المطلوب أو لإحداهما، فإن كان مبيناً لإحداهما فإما الصغرى أو الكبري، فإن كانت الصغرى فهو أن تقضي بقياس المقدمة ثم تدرج منه إلى قياس المطلوب وإن بعد – ونظيره إذا كان المطلوب مثلاً كل "ب" "ج" ومقدمتاه كل "ب" "أ" وكل "أ" "ج" والمبين منهما بالقياس مثلاً كل "ب" "أ" فنقول كل "ب" "د" وكل "د" "أ" فكل "ب" "أ" وكل "أ" "ج" فكل "ب" "ج" فالنتائج زوج ومقدمات كل واحدة منهما زوج، وإن كانت مفردة فليس إلا لزيادة مقدمته على النحو المذكور سلف، أو لنقصان مقدمة إما للحذر للتنبيه على كذبهما عند التصريح بها، أو للاكتفاء بشهرتها عن التصريح بها أول ظهورها عند انتظام مقدمتي قياسها، كما في المقدمة الصغرى في قياس المطلوب السابق، كما ذكره بالنسبة إلى مقدمتيها، فعلى هذا ما صرح فيه بالنتائج فيسمى قياساً موصولاً وما أهمل فيه النتائج فيسمى قياساً مفصولاً
Speech, as is said: Zayd the tall, the bulky is brave, and every brave is daring, so Zayd the tall, the bulky is daring. But the entry of 'tall' and 'bulky' into the speech is not as parts of the syllogism, but for embellishing our saying that Zayd is brave. And from it is for clarification and making speech clear, such as giving examples, citations, and other things. As for what is for clarifying the two premises or one of them, either it is in syllogistic form or not in syllogistic form. In both cases, the syllogism that clarifies the desired conclusion along with the addition of the syllogism clarifying its premise or its two premises is called a compound syllogism, not that it is a single unified syllogism clarifying one desired conclusion, but multiple syllogisms that precede to clarify one desired conclusion, and the syllogism that clarifies the desired conclusion essentially is only one. That either occurs in categoricals or conditionals. If in categoricals, then the syllogism added to the desired syllogism is either clarifying both premises of the desired syllogism or one of them. If it clarifies one of them, then either the minor or the major. If it is the minor, then you judge by a syllogism of the premise, then proceed from it to the desired syllogism, even if far. Its analogue: if the desired conclusion is, for example, every B is C, and its two premises are every B is A and every A is C, and the one clarified among them by a syllogism is, for example, 'every B is A', so we say: every B is D and every D is A, so every B is A, and every A is C, so every B is C. So the conclusions are even, and the premises each are even. And if they are odd, it is only for an increase in its premise in the manner mentioned previously, or for a decrease in a premise either for caution to warn of its falsity when stated explicitly, or for sufficiency by its fame from stating it explicitly, or its appearance when arranging the two premises of its syllogism, as in the minor premise in the syllogism of the previous desired conclusion, as he mentioned regarding its two premises. So on this basis, what is explicit in the conclusions is called a 'connected syllogism', and what omits the conclusions is called a 'separated syllogism'.
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage defines the compound syllogism (qiyas murakkab) and distinguishes its two types: connected (muttasil) and separated (mafusul). The author begins by illustrating that not every part of an argument is a syllogistic element; some parts are for embellishment or clarification, like describing Zayd as tall and bulky to support the claim that he is brave. Then the focus shifts to what actually clarifies premises. When a syllogism is used to prove a premise of another syllogism, the combination is a compound syllogism. The author explains that a compound syllogism is not a single unified syllogism; rather, multiple syllogisms work together to prove a single desired conclusion, with only one of them directly yielding that conclusion. This occurs in both categorical (iqtirani) and conditional (istithna'i) forms. In categoricals, the additional syllogism may prove one or both premises of the main syllogism. If it proves the minor premise, one constructs a syllogism for that premise and then proceeds to the main syllogism. The author gives an example with letters B, A, C, D: to prove that every B is C (minor premise: every B is A, major: every A is C), if the minor premise needs proof, one uses an intermediate term D to show every B is D and every D is A, hence every B is A, then the main syllogism yields every B is C. The number of premises and conclusions may vary. Then the author introduces the key distinction: if all intermediate conclusions are explicitly stated, the compound syllogism is called 'muttasil' (connected); if some intermediate conclusions are omitted (either for caution against falsehood, or because they are well-known, or because they become obvious when the premises are arranged), it is called 'mafusul' (separated). This classification is crucial for understanding how logicians structure complex arguments.

Key Terms
قياس مركب (qiyas murakkab): A compound syllogism formed by adding one or more syllogisms to prove the premises of the main syllogism that yields the desired conclusion. [Note: Generally stable across the tradition; sometimes used interchangeably with 'syllogism with multiple middle terms'.]
قياس موصول (qiyas muttasil): A compound syllogism where all intermediate conclusions are explicitly stated in the argument. [Note: In some later texts, 'muttasil' can also refer to a conditional syllogism; here it refers to connected compound categorical syllogism.]
قياس مفصول (qiyas mafusul): A compound syllogism where some intermediate conclusions are omitted, either because they are obvious, well-known, or to avoid stating false premises explicitly. [Note: Less common term; some authors use 'maqhūl' or 'maḥdhūf' for omitted-premise arguments.]
مقدمة (muqaddima): A premise in a syllogism; can be minor (ṣughrā) or major (kubrā). [Note: Stable term.]
نتيجة (natīja): The conclusion of a syllogism; here used both for the final desired conclusion and intermediate conclusions. [Note: Stable.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) In muttasil all intermediate conclusions are explicitly stated, while in mafusul some are omitted.
C) Muttasil uses only categorical syllogisms, while mafusul uses conditionals.
D) Muttasil is used for demonstrative proofs, while mafusul is for rhetorical arguments.

Key Takeaway
A compound syllogism is a series of syllogisms; it is 'connected' when all intermediate conclusions are explicit, and 'separated' when some are omitted.

The Syllogism

Distinguishing Connected and Separated Compound Syllogisms
Author exemplifies and analyzes connected vs. separated compound syllogisms with numerical rules.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between connected (muttasil) and separated (mafusul) compound syllogisms and apply the parity rules for premises and terms.

Core Idea
Premise-term parity reveals whether a compound syllogism is connected or separated.

The author provides concrete examples using categorical terms (B, D, A, C) to illustrate the two types of compound syllogisms. By analyzing the number of premises and terms, you can determine whether the syllogism chain is connected (all conclusions stated) or separated (some omitted) and understand the implications for constructing valid proofs in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the compound syllogism type from the example
Look at the sequence of premises and conclusions. In the passage, the example 'every B is D, every D is A, every A is C, so every B is C' is a separated syllogism because the intermediate conclusion 'every B is A' is not explicitly stated.
In connected type, all intermediate conclusions are stated, so the sequence would include that intermediate step.
2
Count premises and terms in the example
For the given example, count the number of statements before 'so' (premises): there are three (B→D, D→A, A→C). Count the distinct terms: B, D, A, C = four terms. Note the parity: premises = 3 (odd), terms = 4 (even).
In the passage, the author notes that for separated, if premises are odd, terms are even; here premises odd (3) and terms even (4).
3
Apply the parity rule for each type
Remember: in connected (muttasil) compound syllogisms, premises are always even and terms odd. In separated (mafusul), the parity alternates: if premises even then terms odd, if premises odd then terms even. Check the example: premises odd, terms even, so it fits separated.
This rule helps you quickly classify any compound syllogism without reading all conclusions.
4
Determine the number of conclusions
In connected, conclusions are exactly half the number of premises and are explicitly stated (bi-l-fi‘l). In separated, conclusions are only potential (bi-l-quwwa) and the count is less by one. For the example, premises = 3, so half would be 1.5 but actually only one conclusion (the final) is stated, and the intermediate is potential, confirming separated.
The author states: 'النتائج في الموصول مذكورة بالفعل، وفي المفصول بالقوة، وأنها على النصف في عدد المقدمات في الموصول، وأقل في الموصول بواحد' – but the last phrase is unclear; separated has fewer actual conclusions.
5
Recognize when syllogisms are independent vs. truly compound
The passage also discusses cases where separate syllogisms are merely juxtaposed (e.g., when proving only one premise). Such sequences do not form a single compound syllogism. Only when one syllogism's conclusion serves as a premise for another does true composition occur. This prevents false claims of composition.
Independence is signaled by lack of shared terms between the syllogisms, as seen in the 'muqaddima kubra' example.
