As for the example of the first, it is as we have mentioned. As for the example of the second, it is as in our saying in the previous example: every B is D and every D is A and every A is C, so every B is C. So the premises of the first are four, and of the second three, and the terms from which the composition is made in both are four, since the terms for the first desired syllogism are three as known. So if one of its two premises is the conclusion of another syllogism, then its two extremes [i.e., of that premise] are the major and minor in that [other] syllogism, and it only increases over the desired syllogism by the middle term. On this basis, every added syllogism, if it is connected, increases two premises and one term; otherwise, one premise and one term. As for the conclusions, they are half the number of premises, but if the syllogism is connected they are actually [stated], and if separated they are potentially. On this, the difference between the connected and the separated is that in the connected the conclusions are mentioned actually, and in the separated potentially, and that they are half the number of premises in the connected, and less by one in the connected [than in separated?], and that in the connected the premises are only even and the terms odd, while in the separated, if the premises are even then the terms are odd, and if the premises are odd then the terms are even, and vice versa. Because every added separated compound syllogism adds a premise and adds a term. If before the addition the premises were even, then its terms were odd; after addition the premises become odd and the terms even. And if they were odd, its terms even; after addition they become even and the terms odd. All this if the syllogism is purely connected or purely separated. As for if it is mixed from both, then what we have indicated of the order, and the manner of difference is variable, and the consideration of that depends on each syllogism. As for if the clarification is for the major premise without the minor, that is as if the desired conclusion is every B is C and its two premises are every B is A and every A is C, and you say: every B is A and every A is D and every D is C and every A is C, so every B is C. So every B is A and every A is C is the syllogism that produces the desired conclusion, and every A is D and every D is A is the syllogism that produces the major premise, and they do not interpenetrate, so no composition; their combination if it occurs is only by way of transgression in speech and the second in wording, and each is independent by itself. As for if the clarification is for both premises, as when we say in the mentioned desired conclusion and its two premises: every B is D and every D is A so every B is A, and every A is D and every D is C so every A is C, so every B is C. So these are three syllogisms: a syllogism for the desired conclusion, which is every B is A and every A is C; a syllogism for the minor premise, which is every B is D and every D is A; and a syllogism for the major premise, which is every A is D and every D is C. And no interpenetration either, so no composition as known. And when the foundation of what we have verified is established, the desired conclusion if it is universal affirmative, then its syllogism and the syllogism of its universal premises are not...
Source pages p. 433

Commentary
This passage provides concrete examples of connected (muttasil) and separated (mafusul) compound syllogisms and analyzes their numerical structure. The author first gives an example of a separated syllogism: stating premises 'every B is D', 'every D is A', 'every A is C', and concluding 'every B is C' without explicitly stating the intermediate conclusion 'every B is A' (which would be the result of the first two premises). In this example, the premises are three (odd) and the terms (B, D, A, C) are four. In contrast, the connected version would have four premises (even) because the intermediate conclusion is stated. The author then explains that in any compound syllogism, when a syllogism is added to prove a premise of the main syllogism, it introduces one new term (the middle term of that added syllogism) and either two premises (if connected, because the conclusion is stated) or one premise (if separated, because the conclusion is omitted). The number of conclusions is half the number of premises (rounded?) — in a connected syllogism, conclusions are actual and exactly half the premises; in a separated syllogism, conclusions are potential (since some are omitted) and the count differs. The numerical parity rule: in connected compound syllogisms, premises are always even and terms odd. In separated ones, premises and terms alternate parity depending on how many syllogisms are added. The author then illustrates two special cases: (1) when only the major premise needs proof, the added syllogism stands independently without composing a single compound syllogism with the main one; they are just juxtaposed. (2) When both premises need proof, three distinct syllogisms are formed, each independent, without actual composition. The passage ends with a note about universal affirmative conclusions, but the text cuts off. This detailed numeric analysis helps the logician construct valid chains of syllogisms and understand when a chain is genuinely compound versus merely sequential.

مفصول
حد أوسط
نتيجة

Compound Syllogisms: Conjunctive vs. Disjunctive
Author defines compound syllogisms and distinguishes conjunctive from disjunctive types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish conjunctive from disjunctive compound syllogisms and apply the rules for premises, terms, and conclusions.

Core Idea
In compound syllogisms, conclusions are half the premises; conjunctive makes them actual, disjunctive potential.

Compound syllogisms are formed by chaining simple syllogisms. The author distinguishes two types: al-mawṣūl (conjunctive) where intermediate conclusions are explicitly stated, and al-mafṣūl (disjunctive) where they remain potential. Understanding these distinctions is essential for analyzing complex arguments in Islamic logic, such as multi-step proofs in uṣūl al-fiqh or kalām.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Read the definitions: in mawṣūl, the conclusions of component syllogisms are mentioned explicitly (bi-l-fiʿl); in mafṣūl, they are only potential (bi-l-quwwa).
2
Count premises and terms per added syllogism
Each added syllogism adds two premises and one term if conjunctive, or one premise and one term if disjunctive.
This rule affects the total count in the compound.
3
Compute the number of conclusions
Conclusions are half the number of premises. In mawṣūl they are actual; in mafṣūl they are potential.
Half of an even number is an integer; half of an odd number is fractional, so potential conclusions may not be integers.
4
Determine parity rules
In mawṣūl, premises are always even and terms odd. In mafṣūl, the parity alternates with each addition: if premises were even, after adding one premise they become odd and terms become even, and vice versa.
This holds for pure types; mixed cases follow a different order.
5
Apply to an example
Consider a mawṣūl compound: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden. Every nabīdh is an intoxicant. Therefore, nabīdh is forbidden. Every forbidden thing is unlawful to sell. Nabīdh is forbidden. Therefore, nabīdh is unlawful to sell.' This has two component syllogisms, 4 premises, and 3 terms? Actually, count the terms: intoxicant, forbidden, nabīdh, unlawful to sell? That's 4 terms. But the rule says mawṣūl terms should be odd. The apparent discrepancy is resolved when you realize the middle term 'forbidden' appears in both syllogisms, so total distinct terms: intoxicant, forbidden, nabīdh, unlawful to sell = 4 (even). The text's rule likely refers to each individual component syllogism (each has 2 premises and 3 terms). For the overall compound, the parity alternates with the number of components. Study the text carefully.
The passage may have textual ambiguities; focus on the general distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الثالث: في القياسات المركبة وإشباع القول فيها. فحداهما هما الأكبر والأصغر في ذلك القياس، وإنما يزيد على قياس المطلوب بالحدّ الأوسط، وعلى هذا كل ما زاد قياس فإن كان موضوعاً زاد مقدمتين وحداً واحداً، وإلا فمقدمة وحداً واحداً، وأما النتائج فعلى النصف من عدد المقدمات، لكن إن كان القياس موضوعاً فبالفعل، وإن كان مفصولاً بالقوة، فعلى هذا مفارقة الموصول للمفصول في أن النتائج في الموصول مذكورة بالفعل، وفي المفصول بالقوة، وأنها على النصف في عدد المقدمات في الموصول، وأقل في الموصول بواحد، وأنه لا تكون المقدمات في الموصول إلا أزواجاً والحدود أفراداً وفي المفصول إن كانت أزواجاً فالحدود أفراد، وإن كانت أفراداً فالحدود أزواج وبالعكس؛ لأنه كل ما زاد القياس المركب المفصول مقدمة زاد حداً، فإن كان قبل الزيادة مقدماته شفعاً فحدوده وتر، فبعد الزيادة صارت المقدمات وتراً والحدود شفعاً، وإن كانت وتراً فحدوده شفع، فبعد الزيادة صارت شفعاً والحدود وتراً، هذا كله إن كان القياس موصولاً أو مفصولاً صرفاً، وأما إن كان مختلطاً منهما فما أشرنا إليه من الترتيب، ووجه الفرق بينهما مختلف واعتبار ذلك بحسب كل قياس يسير.
The third art: on compound syllogisms and thoroughly discussing them. The two [extremes] of them are the major and minor in that syllogism, and it increases over the syllogism of the desired [conclusion] by the middle term. Based on this, every added syllogism, if it is conjunctive (موصول), it adds two premises and one term; otherwise, it adds one premise and one term. As for the conclusions, they are half the number of premises, but if the syllogism is conjunctive, then actually, and if disjunctive, then potentially. According to this, the difference between the conjunctive and the disjunctive is that in the conjunctive the conclusions are mentioned actually, and in the disjunctive potentially, and they are half the number of premises in the conjunctive, and one less in the conjunctive [sic], and that the premises in the conjunctive are only even and the terms odd, and in the disjunctive, if they [premises] are even, then the terms are odd, and if they are odd, then the terms are even, and vice versa; because every time a compound disjunctive syllogism adds a premise, it adds a term. So if before the addition its premises were even, its terms were odd; after the addition, the premises become odd and the terms even; and if they were odd, its terms even; after the addition, they become even and the terms odd. All this if the syllogism is purely conjunctive or purely disjunctive. But if it is mixed from both, then [it follows] the order we indicated, and the manner of the difference between them is varying and the consideration of that is according to each syllogism, easily.

Commentary
This passage defines compound syllogisms and distinguishes two types: conjunctive (موصول) and disjunctive (مفصول). The author begins by stating that the extremes (major and minor terms) are present. Each added syllogism increases the number of premises and terms: if conjunctive, it adds two premises and one term; if disjunctive, one premise and one term. The number of conclusions is half the premises, but in the conjunctive type the conclusions are explicitly stated (actual), while in the disjunctive they are potential. The author then details the parity of premises and terms: in conjunctive, premises are always even, terms odd; in disjunctive, the parity alternates with each addition. For example, if before adding a premise the number of premises was even, after addition they become odd and terms become even, and vice versa. This holds for pure types; mixed cases follow a different ordering. The discussion sets the foundation for understanding compound syllogisms in later examples.

Proving the Major Premise with an Independent Syllogism
Author illustrates proving the major premise via an additional syllogism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a compound categorical syllogism where the major premise is derived via an additional independent syllogism, and identify that the component syllogisms remain logically separate.

Core Idea
Compound syllogisms stack proofs without merging into a single logical structure.

In complex arguments, we often need to prove a premise used in a syllogism. This lesson explores how to do so by adding a separate syllogism that proves the needed premise, without mixing the two syllogisms into one. The author illustrates this with a categorical example, showing that the two syllogisms are independent and any conjunction is merely verbal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the desired conclusion and its original premises
The desired conclusion is 'Every B is C'. The original syllogism uses 'Every B is A' and 'Every A is C'. However, 'Every A is C' requires proof.
2
Find the additional syllogism that proves the major premise
The author introduces term D to form a new syllogism: 'Every A is D' and 'Every D is C'. This yields the needed premise 'Every A is C'.
The new syllogism is independent of the original one.
3
Note the independence of the two syllogisms
The author states there is no interpenetration (lā tarkīb) between them; each syllogism is independent (istiglāl bi-nafsihi). Any appearance of combination is merely in wording, not in logical structure.
4
Apply the structure to an Islamic example
Construct a compound syllogism to prove that nabidh (date drink) is forbidden. First syllogism: 'Every intoxicant is forbidden; nabidh is an intoxicant; therefore nabidh is forbidden.' The major premise 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' is already known. The minor premise 'nabidh is an intoxicant' can be proven by a second syllogism: 'Every drink that causes intoxication is an intoxicant; nabidh causes intoxication; therefore nabidh is an intoxicant.' Notice the two syllogisms remain independent.

The author emphasizes that this separate syllogism does not merge with the original; they are independent, and any conjunction is merely verbal, not logical composition. This illustrates the concept of a compound syllogism where one premise is derived through a separate argument.

Proving Both Premises in a Compound Syllogism
Author demonstrates how to prove both the minor and major premises using separate syllogisms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a compound categorical syllogism where both premises are proven by separate syllogisms and verify that the proofs remain independent.

Core Idea
A compound syllogism with proven premises is three syllogisms, not one.

In the previous lesson, we saw how a single premise can be proven by another syllogism. Now the author extends this: both premises of the main syllogism may require proof. This creates a chain of three distinct syllogisms, each with its own premises and conclusion. Understanding this structure prevents the error of thinking they merge into a single composite syllogism.

Guided Path
1
Identify the desired conclusion
The author gives the final claim we want to prove: 'Every B is C'. This is the conclusion of the main syllogism.
Throughout, B, D, A, and C are placeholders for terms (e.g., categories or classes).
2
Extract the two premises of the main syllogism
The main syllogism uses 'Every B is A' (minor premise) and 'Every A is C' (major premise) to derive 'Every B is C'.
The minor premise contains the subject of the conclusion (B); the major premise contains the predicate (C).
3
Find the proof for the minor premise
The minor premise 'Every B is A' is itself proven by another syllogism: 'Every B is D' and 'Every D is A' imply 'Every B is A'.
This is a first-figure syllogism (Barbara).
4
Find the proof for the major premise
Similarly, the major premise 'Every A is C' is proven by 'Every A is D' and 'Every D is C', yielding 'Every A is C'.
This is also a first-figure syllogism.
5
Verify that the three syllogisms are independent
The author stresses 'there is no interpenetration' (لا تداخل). Each syllogism has its own premises and conclusion; they do not overlap or compose into a single syllogism.
The chain is sequential, not composite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

As for if the explanation is for both premises, as when we say in the mentioned desired [conclusion] and its two premises: 'Every B is D and every D is A' so 'Every B is A', and 'Every A is D and every D is C' so 'Every A is C', so 'Every B is C' — these are three syllogisms: a syllogism for the desired [conclusion] which is 'Every B is A and every A is C', and a syllogism for the minor premise which is 'Every B is D and every D is A', and a syllogism for the major premise which is 'Every A is D and every D is C'. There is no interpenetration either, so no composition, as known.

Commentary
This passage extends the previous example by proving both premises. The desired conclusion 'Every B is C' now has an intermediate proof: first the minor premise 'Every B is A' is proven via 'Every B is D' and 'Every D is A'; second, the major premise 'Every A is C' is proven via 'Every A is D' and 'Every D is C'. The original syllogism for the conclusion uses 'Every B is A' and 'Every A is C'. The author stresses that these three syllogisms do not interpenetrate; they remain separate. This illustrates a chain of reasoning without logical composition, i.e., the compound is not a single syllogism but a sequence.

Moods for Universal Affirmative and Negative Conclusions
Author lists possible figures and moods to prove universal affirmative and negative conclusions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which figures and moods can prove universal affirmative and universal negative conclusions in compound syllogisms, and apply these restrictions to evaluate compound syllogisms.

Core Idea
Universal affirmative conclusions require the first mood of the first figure; universal negatives have more options but with dependencies.

In compound syllogisms, the mood of the syllogism determines what type of conclusion it can prove. This passage from Daqaiq al-Haqaiq specifies precisely which figures and moods are valid for universal affirmative and universal negative conclusions. Mastering these restrictions is essential for constructing and evaluating compound syllogisms, which are the backbone of advanced theological and juristic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion type
The passage discusses two cases: when the desired conclusion (al-maṭlūb) is a universal affirmative (kullī mūjib) and when it is a universal negative (kullī sālib). Note which case is being treated.
2
List valid moods for universal affirmative
According to the text, a universal affirmative conclusion can only be proven by the first conjunction (al-iqtirān al-awwal) of the first figure. This mood consists of two universal affirmative premises leading to a universal affirmative conclusion. No other mood works.
This makes universal affirmative conclusions rarer in compound syllogisms.
3
List valid moods for universal negative
The text gives four options: the second mood of the first figure, the first and second moods of the second figure, and the first mood of the fourth figure. These are the only possible moods for a universal negative conclusion.
The second mood of the first figure has a special condition (next step).
4
Understand the special condition for second mood of first figure
If the universal negative syllogism uses the second mood of the first figure, then its two affirmative premises must themselves be proven by a syllogism in the first mood of the first figure. The negative premise is proven similarly to the conclusion itself.
This shows the interdependence of moods in compound syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعند تمهد ما حققناه فالمطلوب إن كان كلياً موجباً فقياسه وقياس كلي مقدمتيه لا يكون من غير الاقتران الأول من الشكل الأول، ولذلك كان أندر وقوعاً من غيره، وإن كان كلياً سالباً فقياسه قد يكون من الاقتران الثاني من الشكل الأول، ومن الأول والثاني من الثاني، ومن الأول من الرابع، فإن كان من الثاني من الشكل الأول فقياس مقدمتيه الموجبة ليس يكون من غير الشكل الأول من الاقتران الأول، وأما السالبة فبيانها قد يكون بمثل ما تبين به المطلوب نفسه، وكذا إن كان البيان بما من الاقترانات.
And when the foundation of what we have verified is established, then the desired [conclusion] if it is universal affirmative, its syllogism and the syllogism of its two universal premises cannot be other than the first conjunction of the first figure, and therefore it occurs more rarely than others. If it is universal negative, its syllogism may be from the second conjunction of the first figure, and from the first and second of the second, and from the first of the fourth. If it is from the second of the first figure, then the syllogism of its two affirmative premises cannot be other than the first figure from the first conjunction. As for the negative [premise], its explanation may be similar to what the desired [conclusion] itself is explained by. Similarly if the explanation is by what of the conjunctions [?].

Commentary
This passage begins the detailed enumeration of the moods (اقترانات) that can prove each type of categorical proposition when the compound syllogism itself is of a certain mood. For a universal affirmative conclusion (كلية موجبة), the syllogism and the proof of its two universal premises can only be from the first mood of the first figure, making it the rarest. For a universal negative conclusion (كلية سالبة), possible moods include: second mood of the first figure, first and second of the second figure, and first of the fourth figure. If the universal negative syllogism is from the second mood of the first figure, then the proof of its two affirmative premises must be from the first mood of the first figure, while the negative premise is proven similarly to the conclusion itself. This sets up the rules for how to construct compound syllogisms for these types.

Moods for Particular Affirmative Conclusions in Compound Syllogisms
Moods for particular affirmative conclusions enumerated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the valid moods for a particular affirmative conclusion and describe the procedure for proving premises in each mood.

Core Idea
The proof of premises in a compound syllogism depends on the mood used for the conclusion.

This passage from Daqaiq al-Haqaiq teaches us that not every syllogistic mood can produce a particular affirmative conclusion. Even after identifying the correct mood, we must ensure that each premise can be justified in a compound proof. The author enumerates the possible moods from the four figures and then gives specific rules for proving the premises. Understanding this helps us construct sound multi-step arguments in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion type
Note that the passage starts with 'If it is particular affirmative' (جزئي موجب). A particular affirmative proposition asserts that some members of a subject have a predicate, e.g., 'Some Fiqh scholars are righteous'. This is the conclusion we aim to prove.
Particular affirmative is one of the four categorical proposition types.
2
List the enumerated moods
The author lists five moods: third mood of the first figure, first mood of the second figure, third mood of the third figure, and second and third moods of the fourth figure. Write these down and note the figure and mood number.
The numbering of moods may differ from later classifications; the author's enumeration is specific to this text.
3
Parse the proof procedures
For each mood, the passage gives a rule for proving its premises. For example, for the third mood of the first figure, the negative premise is explained like a universal negative, and the particular affirmative premise is explained like the conclusion itself. For the first mood of the second figure, both premises are proved only by the first mood of the first figure. Summarize each rule.
Compound syllogisms require proving the premises by separate syllogisms.
4
Apply to an Islamic example

Author enumerates possible moods for proving particular negative conclusions (incomplete).

The proof method depends on the mood, not just the conclusion type.

This passage from Daqaiq al-Haqaiq details the complex rules for constructing compound syllogisms that yield a particular negative conclusion. Understanding these rules is essential for building valid arguments in Islamic logic. We will walk through the enumeration and learn how to apply the proof methods to specific moods.

1
Identify the conclusion type
The passage specifies that the conclusion is a particular negative (جزئي سالب). This means the conclusion is of the form 'Some A is not B'.
2

These are the moods that can yield a particular negative conclusion.

For each mood, the author explains how to prove the minor and major premises. For example, for the fourth mood of the first figure, the minor premise is proven like a particular affirmative, and the major premise is proven like a universal negative.

Then we prove each premise according to its type.

Proving a Universal Negative Proposition Using the Four Figures
Author lists moods that prove a universal negative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the moods from Figures I, II, and IV that can prove a universal negative conclusion, and construct a valid syllogism from one of those moods using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
A universal negative can be proven by three distinct moods across three figures.

The author begins with universal negative (kulli salib) and states it can be proven by the second mood of the first figure, the first and second moods of the second figure, and the first mood of the fourth figure. This shows that multiple deductive paths exist for the same conclusion. In this lesson, we will parse the classification and apply it to a real Islamic legal example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion type
Look for the phrase 'كلياً سالباً' (universal negative) in the Arabic passage. The author then lists the moods that can prove it.
2
Extract the list of moods
The text says: 'من الاقتران الثاني من الشكل الأول، ومن الأول والثاني من الثاني، ومن الأول من الرابع'. This gives three options: second mood of first figure, first and second moods of second figure, first mood of fourth figure.
Some moods may require additional premise proofs, as noted for the second mood of the first figure.
3
Construct an example using one mood
Choose the first mood of the second figure (Cesare). Example: 'No intoxicant is permissible' (major), 'All khamr is intoxicant' (minor), therefore 'No khamr is permissible'. Verify that this is a universal negative conclusion.
This mood is valid: EAE in the second figure.
4
Check the premise proof condition
If using the second mood of the first figure, the author notes that its two affirmative premises must be proven using the first mood of the first figure. For other moods, no such condition is mentioned in this passage.
This shows the recursive nature: some proofs require proving premises first.

It occurs other than from the first mood of the first figure, and therefore it is rarer in occurrence than others. And if it is universal negative, then its syllogism may be from the second mood of the first figure, and from the first and second of the second, and from the first of the fourth. If it is from the second of the first figure, then the syllogism of its two affirmative premises does not occur except from the first figure of the first mood. As for the negative, its proof may be by means similar to that by which the desired [conclusion] itself is proven. Similarly, if the proof is by means of what [comes] from the moods. And if it is particular affirmative, then its syllogism may be from the third mood of the first figure, and the first of the second, and the third of the third, and the second and third of the fourth. If it is from the third of the first, then proof of its negative premise is by that by which the universal negative is proven from the moods, and proof of the affirmative particular is by something similar to that by which the desired [conclusion] itself is proven. And if it is from the fifth of the third, then proof of its minor premise is by that by which the universal affirmative is proven, and the major by that by which the desired [conclusion] itself is proven. And if it is from the sixth of the third, then proof of the minor premise is by that by which the particular affirmative is proven, and the major by that by which the universal negative is proven. And if it is from the fourth of the fourth, then proof of the minor premise is by that by which the universal affirmative is proven, and the major by that by which the universal negative is proven. And if it is from the fifth of the fourth, then proof of the minor premise is by that by which the particular affirmative is proven, and the major by that by which the universal negative is proven. And it is upon you to consider examples of all by yourself, and upon this, its consideration in hypothetical syllogisms is not hidden.
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage is a technical classification of how to prove each of the four types of categorical propositions (universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative) using the four figures of the categorical syllogism and their various moods (اقتران). The author first discusses the universal negative: it can be proven using the second mood of the first figure, the first and second moods of the second figure, or the first mood of the fourth figure. He then notes that if it is from the second mood of the first figure, the syllogism's two affirmative premises must come from the first figure's first mood. The negative premise is proven similarly to proving the conclusion itself. Next, the particular affirmative can be proven using the third mood of the first figure, first mood of the second, third mood of the third, and second and third moods of the fourth. For each case, the author specifies how the premises are proven. For example, if from the third mood of the first figure, the negative premise is proven like a universal negative, and the affirmative particular like the conclusion itself. Then he covers the particular negative, listing many possibilities: fourth mood of the first, third and fourth of the second, fourth, fifth, sixth of the third, fourth and fifth of the fourth. For each, he details how to prove the minor and major premises. The passage ends with an exhortation for the reader to work out examples independently, and notes that the same considerations apply to hypothetical syllogisms. This text is a dense reference for the possible deductive pathways in Aristotelian-Avicennan logic.

Circular Syllogism: Definition and Legitimate Use
Defines circular syllogism, explains its use, and begins classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define circular syllogism (al-dawr) and distinguish its legitimate scientific use from its sophistical application.

Core Idea
Circular reasoning can be valid when it demonstrates causal dependence, not just fallacy.

The author begins the fourth art by defining circular syllogism. Although often used in sophistry, he argues it has a legitimate place in the sciences when we need to prove causation (lamiyya) rather than mere existence (inniyya). Let's examine the definition and the initial classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
Locate the phrase 'عبارة عن أخذ النتيجة مع عكس إحدى مقدمتي قياسها لاستنتاج المقدمة الأخرى'. This is the precise definition of circular syllogism. Note the components: conclusion, converse of one premise, and the other premise as product.
2
Contrast the two uses
The author says it is mostly used in sophistry (مغالطة) and examination (امتحان), but also sometimes in sciences (علوم) to produce a syllogism on the manner of proof of cause (لمية). Understand the difference: sophistry uses it to deceive, science uses it to show causal dependency.
The terms لمية (lamiyya) and إنية (inniyya) are key: the former is proof of the cause, the latter proof of existence.
3
Recognize the classification
The author classifies circular syllogism into categorical (اقتراني) and conditional (استثنائي). This is the first division. Notice that he then focuses on the categorical type with further subdivisions.
The conditional type is mentioned but not elaborated in this passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الرابع: في قياس الدور وعكس القياس. نقول أنه وإن كان أكثر الانتفاع بهذين القياسين إنما هو في المغالطة والامتحان لكنه لما كانا عارضين للقياس من جهة ما هو قياس وكان القياس عين موضوعها الذي يبحث فيه عنها، وجب أن يكون النظر فيهما في علم القياس. أما الدور فهو عبارة عن أخذ النتيجة مع عكس إحدى مقدمتي قياسها لاستنتاج المقدمة الأخرى، وهو وإن كان أكثر استعماله للمغالطة والامتحان حيث نتبين فيه توقف كل واحد من النتيجة والمقدمة على الأخرى في البيان، فربما استعمل في العلوم لأن يكون القياس منتجاً على سبيل اللميّة، حيث كان لا ينتج إلا على سبيل الإنية كما ستعلمه، وعند ذلك فالقياس الدائر إما اقتراني أو استثنائي. فإن كان اقترانياً فمقدمتاه إما كليتان أو أن إحداهما جزئية. فإن كانتا كليتين فإما موجبتان أو إن إحداهما سالبة. فإن كانتا موجبتين فإما من الشكل الأول أو الثاني أو الثالث أو الرابع. فإن كان من الأول فاقتران النتيجة بعكس إحدى المقدمتين من غير تغيير كمية تنتج عن المقدمة الأخرى. وذلك كما لو قيل: كل إنسان ضاحك، وكل ضاحك كاتب، فكل إنسان كاتب. فقلت: كل ضاحك إنسان، وكل إنسان كاتب، أنتج كل ضاحك كاتب، وهو عين المقدمة الكبرى. ولو قلت: وكل إنسان كاتب، وكل كاتب ضاحك، أنتج كل إنسان ضاحك، وهو عين المقدمة الصغرى. وأما إن كان من غير الشكل الأول فلا يكون من غير المختلطات كما سبق، وإذ ذاك فلا يتهيأ منه دور؛ إذ الدور لا يتم بغير عكس إحدى المقدمتين من غير تغيير كمية. ومهما انعكست إحدى المقدمتين بنفسها صار الحد الأوسط مساوياً لما فيها من أحد الطرفين المختلفين من غير عموم ولا خصوص، عند ذلك فيتعذر إثباته للطرف الآخر؛ لضرورة أن النسبة بين الطرفين بالسلب وأن الحد الأوسط مساءٍ [؟] في الحمل لأحدهما.
The Fourth Art: On Circular Syllogism and its Conversion. We say: Although the benefit of these two syllogisms is mostly in sophistry and examination, yet since they are incidental to syllogism as such, and syllogism is the subject in which they are investigated, it is necessary to consider them in the science of syllogism. As for circular syllogism (al-dawr), it consists in taking the conclusion together with the converse of one of its premises to deduce the other premise. And although its use is mostly for sophistry and examination, where we show the dependence of each of the conclusion and premise on the other in proof, yet it is sometimes used in sciences so that the syllogism may be productive in the manner of proof of cause (لمية), when it would otherwise only be productive in the manner of proof of existence (إنية), as you will learn. Given that, the circular syllogism is either categorical (iqtirani) or conditional (istithna'i). If it is categorical, its two premises are either both universal or one is particular. If both are universal, they are either both affirmative or one is negative. If both are affirmative, then it is either of the first, second, third, or fourth figure. If it is of the first figure, then conjoining the conclusion with the converse of one of the premises without changing quantity produces the other premise. For example: 'Every human is laughing', 'Every laughing is writing', therefore 'Every human is writing'. Then you say: 'Every laughing is human', 'Every human is writing', it produces 'Every laughing is writing', which is exactly the major premise. And if you said: 'Every human is writing', 'Every writing is laughing', it produces 'Every human is laughing', which is exactly the minor premise. As for when it is not of the first figure, it cannot be without mixed syllogisms as previously mentioned; then a circle cannot be formed, because the circle is not completed without the converse of one premise without changing quantity. Whenever one of the premises is converted exactly as it is, the middle term becomes equal to one of the two different terms in it, without generality or particularity; then it becomes impossible to prove it for the other term, because necessarily the relation between the two terms is negative and the middle term is equal in attribution to one of them.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage opens the fourth art (الفن الرابع) of the book, which deals with circular syllogism (قياس الدور) and its conversion (عكس القياس). The author first justifies including these topics in the science of logic, noting that although they are mostly used in sophistry and examination, they are incidental to syllogism as such, and therefore must be discussed. He defines circular syllogism as taking the conclusion together with the converse of one premise to deduce the other premise. Despite its common use in fallacious arguments, he points out that it can be used legitimately in the sciences to produce syllogisms that demonstrate causation (لمية) rather than mere existence (إنية). The author then classifies circular syllogism into categorical and conditional types. For the categorical, he breaks it down by quantity and quality of premises, and by figure. He focuses on the first figure with two affirmative universal premises, giving an example: from 'Every human is laughing' and 'Every laughing is writing', we infer 'Every human is writing'. Then, by conjoining this conclusion with the converse of one premise (e.g., 'Every laughing is human' from 'Every human is laughing'), we recover the other premise ('Every laughing is writing'). He explains that only the first figure allows this without changing quantity; other figures require mixed syllogisms and thus cannot form a proper circle. The passage ends by explaining why conversion must not change the quantity: otherwise the middle term becomes equal to one extreme, making the proof impossible because the relation becomes negative.

عكس القياس
اقتراني
استثنائي

Circular syllogism is defined as taking the conclusion with the converse of a premise to deduce the other premise, and it can be legitimately used for causal proofs in the sciences.

The Syllogism

Performing Categorical Circular Syllogism in the First Figure
Explains the procedure for categorical circular syllogism in the first figure with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to execute a categorical circular syllogism in the first figure with two universal affirmative premises, recovering either premise.

Core Idea
Only in the first figure can you circle back without changing the quantity of the premises.

The author now illustrates the procedure for categorical circular syllogism using a concrete example: humans, laughing, writing. He shows how conjoining the conclusion with the converse of one premise yields the other premise without changing quantity. Let's walk through the steps and understand why only the first figure works.

Guided Path
1
Start with a valid first-figure syllogism
The author gives: 'كل إنسان ضاحك' (Every human is laughing), 'كل ضاحك كاتب' (Every laughing is writing), therefore 'كل إنسان كاتب' (Every human is writing). Identify this as a Barbara syllogism (AAA-1).
2
Convert one premise
Take the minor premise 'كل إنسان ضاحك' and convert it simply to 'كل ضاحك إنسان' (Every laughing is human). Note that this conversion is valid only because the premises are universal affirmative (A) and in logic, conversion of A is not always valid, but here it is treated as possible because of the specific terms.
The author says 'من غير تغيير كمية' (without changing quantity), meaning the converse remains universal.
3
Conjoin the conclusion with the converse
Take the conclusion 'كل إنسان كاتب' and the converse of the minor 'كل ضاحك إنسان' to form a new syllogism: from 'كل ضاحك إنسان' and 'كل إنسان كاتب', we deduce 'كل ضاحك كاتب' which is exactly the major premise. This recovers the major.
Similarly, you could recover the minor by using the converse of the major.
4
Verify the condition
The author emphasizes that this circle is only possible in the first figure without changing quantity. In other figures, mixed syllogisms are needed, and the quantity must change, which prevents a proper circle.

الفن الرابع: في قياس الدور وعكس القياس. نقول أنه وإن كان أكثر الانتفاع بهذين القياسين إنما هو في المغالطة والامتحان لكنه لما كانا عارضين للقياس من جهة ما هو قياس وكان القياس عين موضوعها الذي يبحث فيه عنها، وجب أن يكون النظر فيهما في علم القياس. أما الدور فهو عبارة عن أخذ النتيجة مع عكس إحدى مقدمتي قياسها لاستنتاج المقدمة الأخرى، وهو وإن كان أكثر استعماله للمغالطة والامتحان حيث نتبين فيه توقف كل واحد من النتيجة والمقدمة على الأخرى في البيان، فربما استعمل في العلوم لأن يكون القياس منتجاً على سبيل اللميّة، حيث كان لا ينتج إلا على سبيل الإنية كما ستعلمه، وعند ذلك فالقياس الدائر إما اقتراني أو استثنائي. فإن كان اقترانياً فمقدمتاه إما كليتان أو أن إحداهما جزئية. فإن كانتا كليتين فإما موجبتان أو إن إحداهما سالبة. فإن كانتا موجبتين فإما من الشكل الأول أو الثاني أو الثالث أو الرابع. فإن كان من الأول فاقتران النتيجة بعكس إحدى المقدمتين من غير تغيير كمية تنتج عن المقدمة الأخرى. وذلك كما لو قيل: كل إنسان ضاحك، وكل ضاحك كاتب، فكل إنسان كاتب. فقلت: كل ضاحك إنسان، وكل إنسان كاتب، أنتج كل ضاحك كاتب، وهو عين المقدمة الكبرى. ولو قلت: وكل إنسان كاتب، وكل كاتب ضاحك، أنتج كل إنسان ضاحك، وهو عين المقدمة الصغرى. وأما إن كان من غير الشكل الأول فلا يكون من غير المختلطات كما سبق، وإذ ذاك فلا يتهيأ منه دور؛ إذ الدور لا يتم بغير عكس إحدى المقدمتين من غير تغيير كمية. ومهما انعكست إحدى المقدمتين بنفسها صار الحد الأوسط مساوياً لما فيها من أحد الطرفين المختلفين من غير عموم ولا خصوص، عند ذلك فيتعذر إثباته للطرف الآخر؛ لضرورة أن النسبة بين الطرفين بالسلب وأن الحد الأوسط مساءٍ [؟] في الحمل لأحدهما.

Definition and Use of Circular Syllogism
Author defines circular syllogism, states its uses, and divides it into categorical and conditional.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define circular syllogism, distinguish its legitimate scientific use from its fallacious use, and identify its two types (categorical and conditional).

Core Idea
Circular syllogism can be a legitimate tool for demonstrating the reason (لمية) when properly constructed, not just a fallacy.

This lesson introduces circular syllogism, a technique often associated with fallacies but which, under proper conditions, can elevate a demonstration from mere fact (إنية) to reasoned fact (لمية). The author justifies its inclusion in logic and provides a clear definition and classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definition
Break down the definition: circular syllogism takes the conclusion together with the converse of one of its premises to deduce the other premise. Identify each component: the conclusion, the converse of a premise, and the deduced premise.
The converse of a premise is a proposition formed by swapping subject and predicate under the rules of conversion.
2
Distinguish the two main uses
The author states that circular syllogism is mostly used in fallacies (مغالطة) and tests (امتحان), but it can also be used legitimately in the sciences to produce demonstration of the reason (لمية).
Fallacious use involves vicious circularity, while legitimate use is controlled and purposeful.
3
Understand the conditions for legitimate use
Legitimate use occurs when a standard syllogism only yields demonstration of the fact (إنية). By applying circular reasoning, the syllogism can be restructured to yield demonstration of the reason (لمية), i.e., showing why the conclusion holds.
This is possible when the converse of a premise is logically equivalent and the conclusion is already established.
4
Classify the types
The author divides circular syllogism into categorical (اقتراني) and conditional (استثنائي). Identify which type of propositions are involved: categorical syllogisms use subject-predicate statements, while conditional syllogisms use if-then or disjunctive statements.
Future lessons will detail each type.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفن الرابع في قياس الدور وعكس القياس. نقول أنه وإن كان أكثر الانتفاع بهذين القياسين إنما هو في المغالطة والامتحان لكنه لما كانا عارضين للقياس من جهة ما هو قياس وكان القياس عين موضوعها الذي يبحث فيه عنها، وجب أن يكون النظر فيهما في علم القياس. أما الدور فهو عبارة عن أخذ النتيجة مع عكس إحدى مقدمتي قياسها لاستنتاج المقدمة الأخرى، وهو وإن كان أكثر استعماله للمغالطة والامتحان حيث نتبين فيه توقف كل واحد من النتيجة والمقدمة على الأخرى في البيان، فربما استعمل في العلوم لأن يكون القياس منتجاً على سبيل اللمية، حيث كان لا ينتج إلا على سبيل الإنية كما ستعلمه، وعند ذلك فالقياس الدائر إما اقتراني أو استثنائي.
The Fourth Art: On Circular Syllogism and Conversion of the Syllogism. We say that although the greatest benefit of these two syllogisms is in fallacy and examination, yet since they are incidental to the syllogism qua syllogism, and the syllogism is the very subject matter in which they are investigated, it is necessary to consider them in the science of syllogism. As for circularity, it is the taking of the conclusion along with the converse of one of its two premises to deduce the other premise. Although its use is mostly for fallacy and examination where we show the dependence of each of the conclusion and the premise on the other in demonstration, it is sometimes used in the sciences so that the syllogism produces a demonstration of the reason (limiyya), when it would otherwise only produce a demonstration of the fact (inniyya), as you will learn. And accordingly, the circular syllogism is either categorical or conditional.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage opens the Fourth Art of the book, dedicated to circular syllogism (دور) and conversion of the syllogism. The author first justifies including this topic in logic: although these two techniques are most often used in fallacies and tests (امتحان), they are nevertheless proper to the study of syllogism because they arise from the nature of syllogism itself. Circular syllogism is defined as taking the conclusion together with the converse of one of its premises in order to deduce the other premise. The author notes its typical use in fallacies, where one demonstrates the mutual dependence of premise and conclusion, but it can also have a legitimate scientific use: it can transform a syllogism that only gives knowledge of the fact (إنية) into one that gives knowledge of the reason (لمية). Finally, the author divides circular syllogism into categorical (اقتراني) and conditional (استثنائي), setting up the detailed discussion to follow.

Key Terms
دور (dawr): Taking the conclusion along with the converse of one of its premises to deduce the other premise, i.e., a circular reasoning that uses the conclusion as part of the proof of a premise. [Note: In later logic, 'دور' often refers to the fallacy of begging the question, but here it is a specific valid technique used in proper conditions.]
اللمية (al-limiyya): Demonstration of the reason or cause (why something is the case), as opposed to mere fact. [Note: Stable term in Aristotelian and Islamic logic for demonstration of the reasoned fact (propter quid).]
الإنية (al-inniyya): Demonstration of the fact (that something is the case) without showing the cause. [Note: Stable term for demonstration of the fact (quia).]
اقتراني (iqtiranī): A syllogism composed of categorical propositions (subject-predicate), as opposed to conditional. [Note: Standard term in Islamic logic for categorical syllogism, distinguishing it from the conditional (istithnā'ī).]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Circular syllogism is the deduction of one premise from the conclusion and the converse of the other premise, and it can be used legitimately to achieve demonstration of the reason (limiyya) rather than just fact (inniyya).

The Syllogism
advanced
10 min
Circular Syllogism with Both Premises Universal Affirmative
Author explains conditions for circular syllogism in categorical form with both premises universal affirmative, examining each figure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine which figure of a categorical syllogism permits a valid circular syllogism when both premises are universal affirmative.

Core Idea
Only the first figure yields a valid circular syllogism with two universal affirmatives.

In this passage, the author examines the possibility of forming a circular syllogism (qiyas da'ir) when both premises are universal affirmative. He systematically checks each figure and shows that only the first figure works. Understanding this helps you see why certain logical operations preserve validity only under specific conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mood and potential figures
The syllogism has both premises universal affirmative (AAA mood). The author checks all four figures. Note that only the first figure normally yields a universal affirmative conclusion from two universal affirmatives.
2
Analyze the first figure procedure
In the first figure, the conclusion is universal affirmative. By converting one premise (without changing quantity, as assumed) and conjoining it with the conclusion, you can derive the other premise. The author provides examples: from 'Every human is laughing' and 'Every laughing is a writer' to get 'Every human is a writer', then the circular steps derive each premise back.
The conversion assumed here is problematic in standard logic (A converts to I, not A). The author assumes a conversion that preserves universality, possibly when terms are co-extensive.
3
Examine the second figure failure
In the second figure, the middle term is predicate in both premises. Converting one premise without changing quantity makes the middle term equal to one extreme, requiring a negative relation to prove the other. But the premises are both affirmative, so the necessary negative conclusion cannot be derived. Hence no circular syllogism.
The author says 'the middle term becomes equal to one of the two differing extremes, without generality or particularity; then it becomes impossible to prove it for the other extreme'.
4
Examine the third and fourth figures failure
In the third and fourth figures, two universal affirmative premises produce only a particular conclusion (because the middle term is not distributed in both premises). A particular conclusion cannot serve as a premise to deduce a universal premise, so circular syllogism fails.
The author states: 'the conclusion from two universal affirmatives in the mentioned figures can only be particular, and a particular cannot produce a universal syllogism'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كانتا كليتين فإما موجبتان أو إن إحداهما سالبة، فإن كانتا موجبتين فإما من الشكل الأول أو الثاني أو الثالث أو الرابع، فإن كان من الأول فاقتران النتيجة بعكس - أي المقدمتين – من غير تغيير كمية تنتج عن المقدمة الأخرى، وذلك كما لو قيل كل إنسان ضاحك وكل ضاحك كاتب فكل إنسان كاتب فقلت كل ضاحك إنسان وكل إنسان كاتب أنتج كل ضاحك كاتب وهو عين المقدمة الكبرى، ولو قلت وكل إنسان كاتب وكل كاتب ضاحك لأنتج كل إنسان ضاحك وهو عين المقدمة الصغرى. وأما إن كان من الشكل الثاني فلا يكون من غير المختلطات كما سبق، وإذ ذاك فلا يتهيأ منه دور؛ إذ الدور لا يتم بغير عكس إحدى المقدمتين من غير تغيير كمية، ومهما انعكست إحدى المقدمتين بنفسها صار الحد الأوسط مساوياً لما فيها من أحد الطرفين المختلفين من غير عموم ولا خصوص عند ذلك فيتعذر إثباته للطرف الآخر؛ لضرورة أن النسبة بين الطرفين بالسلب وأن الحد الأوسط مساوٍ في الحمل لأحدهما. وأما إن كان من الشكل الثالث أو الرابع فليس يتهيأ منه دور؛ إذ كل واحدة من مقدمتي الاقتران فيها لا تكون إلا كليّة، والنتيجة من الموجبتين الكليتين كما عرف من الشكلين المذكورين لا تكون إلا جزئية، وليس يتهيأ من الجزئي قياس منتج للكلي.
If they are both universal, then either both are affirmative or one is negative. If they are both affirmative, then [the syllogism] is either in the first figure, second, third, or fourth. If it is in the first, then conjoining the conclusion with the converse—i.e., the two premises—without changing quantity produces the other premise. For example, if one says: 'Every human is laughing' and 'Every laughing is a writer', so 'Every human is a writer'; then if you say: 'Every laughing is a human' and 'Every human is a writer', it produces 'Every laughing is a writer', which is exactly the major premise. And if you say: 'Every human is a writer' and 'Every writer is laughing', it would produce 'Every human is laughing', which is exactly the minor premise. As for the second figure, it does not occur without mixed premises [as previously]; and so no circular syllogism can be formed from it; for the circular syllogism is not completed except by converting one of the two premises without changing quantity. And whenever one of the two premises is converted by itself, the middle term becomes equal to what is in it of one of the two differing extremes, without generality or particularity; then it becomes impossible to prove it for the other extreme, necessarily because the relation between the two extremes is negative and the middle term is equal in predication to one of them. As for the third or fourth figure, no circular syllogism can be formed from them, because each of the two premises in them can only be universal, and the conclusion from two universal affirmatives in the mentioned figures can only be particular, and a particular cannot produce a universal syllogism.
Source pages p. 437

Commentary
This passage examines the specific case of a categorical circular syllogism where both premises are universal affirmative. The author systematically checks each of the four figures. For the first figure, the procedure works: one takes the conclusion and converts one premise (keeping the same quantity) to derive the other premise. The text provides two examples: one derives the major premise by converting the minor, and the other derives the minor by converting the major (using a further conversion). For the second figure, the author explains that it does not produce a valid circular syllogism without mixed premises (i.e., one premise not universal affirmative), because simply converting one premise without changing quantity makes the middle term equal to one of the extremes, rendering the other relation negative and impossible to prove. For the third and fourth figures, the problem is that with two universal affirmatives, the conclusion is particular (since the middle term is not distributed in both premises), and a particular conclusion cannot serve as a premise to deduce a universal premise. Thus, only the first figure yields a valid circular syllogism in this mood.

Key Terms
الشكل الأول
الحد الأوسط
عكس

A) Because the middle term is not distributed
B) Because the conclusion is particular, and a particular cannot produce a universal
C) Because the premises must be negative
D) Because conversion is not possible in the third figure

Key Takeaway
A circular syllogism with two universal affirmative premises is only possible in the first figure, due to the distribution properties of the middle term and the quantity of the conclusion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Therefore, every prayer is obedient to Allah. Why Circular Syllogism Fails in Figures 3 and 4
Author states that circular syllogism cannot be derived from third or fourth figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a circular syllogism cannot be constructed in the third or fourth figure and identify the key quantifier obstacle.

Core Idea
Circular syllogism fails because the conclusion is particular but the needed premise is universal.

Circular reasoning (dawr) requires that the conclusion of a syllogism be used to prove one of its own premises. This imposes a strict condition on the quantifiers: the conclusion must be as strong as the premise it replaces. The author now examines why this fails specifically in the third and fourth figures of the categorical syllogism. Let us trace the logical constraint.

Guided Path
1
Recall the requirement for circular syllogism
A circular syllogism needs the conclusion to serve as a premise to deduce the other premise. Therefore, the conclusion must have the same or greater logical strength (quantity and quality) as the premise it aims to prove.
2
Examine the premises in figures 3 and 4
In these figures, when both premises are affirmative, they must be universal (kulliyya). This is a known rule of the third and fourth figures.
The author states: 'كل واحدة من مقدمتي الاقتران فيها لا تكون إلا كليّة' – each premise is only universal.
3
Determine the conclusion's quantifier
From two universal affirmatives in these figures, the conclusion is always particular (juz'iyya). This is the distinctive characteristic of the third and fourth figures.
The author says: 'النتيجة من الموجبتين الكليتين لا تكون إلا جزئية' – the conclusion is only particular.
4
Check the possibility of circularity
A particular conclusion cannot serve as a premise to prove a universal premise, because the particular is weaker. Hence, the circular syllogism is impossible.
The author concludes: 'ليس يتهيأ من الجزئي قياس منتج للكلي' – from the particular, a syllogism producing the universal cannot be derived.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما وإن كان من الشكل الثالث أو الرابع فليس يتهيأ منه دور؛ إذ كل واحدة من مقدمتي الاقتران فيها لا تكون إلا كليّة، والنتيجة من الموجبتين الكليتين كما عرف من الشكلين المذكورين لا تكون إلا جزئية، وليس يتهيأ من الجزئي قياس منتج للكلي
As for if it is from the third or fourth figure, a circular syllogism cannot be derived from it; because each one of the two premises of the conjunctive [syllogism] in them is only universal, and the conclusion from two universal affirmatives, as known from the two mentioned figures, is only particular, and from the particular a syllogism producing the universal cannot be derived.
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage explains why a circular syllogism (dawr) is impossible in the third and fourth figures. The author states that in these figures, every premise is universal, but the conclusion from two universal affirmatives is necessarily particular (as known from the rules of these figures). Since a particular conclusion cannot serve as a premise to deduce a universal one, a true circular proof (where the conclusion helps prove the premises) is impossible. The key logical point is that circular reasoning requires the conclusion to have the same quantity as the premise it replaces, but here the conclusion is weaker (particular) while the premise is stronger (universal).

Key Terms
شكل ثالث
شكل رابع
دور
مقدمة كلية
نتيجة جزئية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Circular syllogism is impossible in the third and fourth figures because the conclusion is always particular and cannot prove a universal premise.

The Syllogism
advanced
12 min
Circular Syllogism in the First Figure with Conversion and Necessary Concomitants
Author explains how circular syllogism is possible in the first figure using conversion and necessary conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a circular syllogism in the first figure using conversion of the minor and identify the necessary condition for coupling with the converse of the major to recover the minor via necessary concomitants.

Core Idea
Circular syllogism in the first figure is possible with conversion, but only the minor premise can be recovered; the major requires a special condition with necessary concomitants.

This passage from Daqaiq al-Haqaiq explores how a circular syllogism (dawr) can be attempted in the first figure when one premise is negative. The author first shows that coupling the conclusion with the converse of the minor premise yields the major premise. Then he addresses a more complex case: coupling with the converse of the major normally fails, but if the negation has a special property, taking the necessary concomitant (lazim) of the privation and using a modified proposition (mu'addala) can recover the minor. Understanding these conditions prevents invalid circular reasoning in theological and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original syllogism
Find a valid first-figure categorical syllogism with one negative premise. Example: Every man is laughing (minor), nothing laughing is a horse (major), therefore no man is a horse.
The minor is universal affirmative, the major is universal negative.
2
Couple conclusion with converse of the minor
Convert the minor premise to its converse without changing quantity (assuming coextension). In the example, 'Every man is laughing' becomes 'Every laughing thing is man'. Couple this with the conclusion 'No man is horse' to form a new syllogism. Its conclusion 'Nothing laughing is horse' is the original major.
This works only if the converse is universal, which requires special conditions (e.g., the subject and predicate are coextensive).
3
Test coupling with converse of the major
Now try to recover the minor by coupling the conclusion with the converse of the major. Convert 'Nothing substance is accident' to 'Nothing accident is substance'. Couple with conclusion 'No man is accident'. Both premises are negative, so no conclusion follows.
Two negatives in the first figure produce nothing.
4
Apply the special condition with necessary concomitant
If the negative in the major is such that the predicate is specifically denied of the subject (i.e., the subject cannot have the predicate), then take the necessary concomitant (lazim) of the privation: e.g., 'Everything not accident is substance'. Modify the conclusion: 'No man is accident' becomes 'Every man is not accident' (mu'addala). Then couple this modified conclusion with the necessary concomitant to deduce 'Every man is substance', which is the original minor.
This is not a true circular syllogism because the minor is proven via a necessary concomitant, not the conclusion itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إن كانت إحداهما سالبة فإما من الشكل الأول أو الثاني أو الثالث أو الرابع، فإن كان من الشكل الأول فاقتران النتيجة بعكس الصغرى من غير تغيير كمية، ينتج عن الكبرى وهو كما لو قيل كل إنسان ضاحك ولا شيء من الضاحك (فرس) فـ (لا شيء من الإنسان (فرس فإنه إذا قيل (كل ضاحك إنسان) و(لا شيء من الإنسان (فرس أنتج (لا شيء من الضاحك فرس وهو عين الكبرى.

المقدمة

وأما اقتران النتيجة بعكس الكبرى فاقتران سالب بسالب ولا إنتاج. اللهم إلا أن يكون المسلوب في السالبة خاص السلب عن الموضوع، بحيث يكون وجوده في ما سواه، ولا يتصور سلبه عنه، فإنه إذا أخذ لازم عكس هذا السلب مع اقتران النتيجة به معدولة أمكن استنتاج الموجبة منهما، وذلك كما لو قيل كل إنسان جوهر) و (لا شيء من الجوهر (عرض) فـ (لا شيء من الإنسان (عرض) فإنك إذا قلت (لا) شيء من العرض (جوهر) ثم أخذت لازم هذا السلب وهو كل ما هو غير عرض فهو (جوهر وقرينته بالنتيجة معدولة فقلت كل إنسان فهو غير عرض) و(كل ما هو غير عرض فهو (جوهر) أنتج كل إنسان (جوهر) وهو عين المقدمة الصغرى، لكن ما مثل هذا الدور ليس على النحو الذي يتوقف فيه كل واحد من الشيئين على الآخر، بل النتيجة وإن كانت متوقفة على المقدمة الصغرى فليس بيان الصغرى متوقفاً على عين النتيجة، بل على لازمها، ومن هنا إن قيل أنه ليس بدور، فحق، وأما من غيره فلا.
As for if one of them is negative, then it is either from the first, second, third, or fourth figure. If it is from the first figure, then coupling the conclusion with the converse of the minor without changing quantity produces the major, as when it is said: 'Every man is laughing, and nothing that laughs is a horse' so 'Nothing that is man is a horse' — then if it is said: 'Every laughing thing is man, and nothing that is man is a horse' it produces 'Nothing that laughs is a horse', which is exactly the major premise.

The premise. As for coupling the conclusion with the converse of the major, it is coupling a negative with a negative and produces nothing, unless the negated thing in the negative is specifically denied of the subject, such that its existence is in other than it, and its being denied of it cannot be conceived. For then, if one takes the necessary concomitant of the converse of this privation along with coupling the conclusion with it in a modified way, it becomes possible to deduce the affirmative from them. Example: 'Every man is substance, and nothing that is substance is accident' so 'Nothing that is man is accident'. Then if you say 'Nothing that is accident is substance' and then take the necessary concomitant of this privation, i.e., everything that is not accident is substance, and couple it with the conclusion modified: 'Every man is not accident' and 'Everything not accident is substance', it produces 'Every man is substance', which is exactly the minor premise. But such a circular syllogism is not in the way where each of the two things depends on the other; rather, although the conclusion depends on the minor premise, the proof of the minor does not depend on the conclusion itself but on its necessary concomitant. Hence if it is said that this is not a proper circular syllogism, that is true; but in other cases it is not so.
Source pages p. 438

Commentary
This passage explains how a circular syllogism (dawr) can be attempted in the first figure. The author first considers the case where one premise is negative. If the syllogism is in the first figure, coupling the conclusion with the converse of the minor premise (without changing quantity) yields the major premise. Example: from 'Every man is laughing, nothing laughing is a horse' we conclude 'No man is horse'; by converting the minor 'Every laughing thing is man' and coupling with the same conclusion, we get 'Nothing laughing is horse' which is the original major. However, coupling the conclusion with the converse of the major results in two negatives, which produce nothing — unless the negative has a special property: the negated term cannot exist in the subject. Then by taking the necessary concomitant (lazim) of the privation (i.e., what is not the negated term) and using a modified (mu'adhdhala) proposition, an affirmative conclusion can be derived. Example: 'Every man is substance, nothing substance is accident' yields 'No man is accident'. By converting the major to 'Nothing accident is substance', then taking its necessary concomitant 'Everything not accident is substance', and coupling with the modified conclusion 'Every man is not accident', we deduce 'Every man is substance' — the original minor. But the author notes this is not a true circular syllogism because the proof of the minor uses a necessary concomitant, not the conclusion itself.

Key Terms
عكس الصغرى
لازم
معدولة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first figure, what happens when you couple the conclusion with the converse of the major premise without any special condition?
A) It produces the minor premise
B) It produces the major premise
C) It produces nothing because two negatives cannot yield a conclusion
D) It produces a universal negative

Key Takeaway
Circular syllogism in the first figure is possible by coupling the conclusion with the converse of the minor to get the major, but coupling with the converse of the major requires a necessary concomitant and modified proposition to succeed, and even then it is not a true circular syllogism.

Task 1: Take the valid first-figure syllogism: 'Every believer is righteous, no righteous person is a hypocrite'. Using the method described, couple the conclusion with the converse of the minor to recover the major premise. Write the steps and note any assumptions needed for the conversion.

Circular Reasoning in First and Second Figures
Author explains circular syllogism with examples for first and second figures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify circular reasoning in categorical syllogisms and reconstruct the circular derivation using conversion and modified propositions.

Core Idea
Circular reasoning in syllogisms uses conversion or modification to derive a premise from the conclusion.

In this passage, the author introduces circular reasoning (دور) within categorical syllogisms, specifically in the first and second figures. We will walk through the examples he provides to understand how the conclusion and one premise can be used to derive the other premise, sometimes requiring more than one conversion or the use of modified propositions. This skill is critical for detecting hidden circularity in arguments within kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original syllogistic argument
Locate the initial premises and conclusion. For example, from 'Every human is laughing' and 'No stone is laughing' to 'No human is stone'. Note the mood and figure.
This syllogism is in the second mood of the first figure (Celarent).
2
Apply conversion to the conclusion
Convert the conclusion 'No human is stone' to 'No stone is human'. This is the first step in the circular derivation.
Conversion of a universal negative is simple and preserves truth.
3
Combine converted conclusion with a premise to derive the missing premise
Using the converted conclusion 'No stone is human' as a premise with the original minor 'Every human is laughing', derive the original major 'No stone is laughing' via the second mood.
This shows how the conclusion and one premise can reproduce the other premise.
4
Recognize the circularity
Note that the derived premise is identical to a premise already used, creating a circle. The author also shows that sometimes more than one conversion is needed, or modified propositions are required.
Circularity is not always obvious; it may involve two conversions or modified (معدولة) propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنتاج السالبة لا يكون إلا بالاقتران الثاني من الشكل الأول، ولا بد فيه من عكس النتيجة، وهو كما لو قيل: كل إنسان ضاحك، ولا شيء من الحجر ضاحك، فلا شيء من الإنسان حجر. فإنك إذا قلت: كل ضاحك إنسان، ولا شيء من الإنسان حجر، أنتج من الاقتران الثاني من الشكل الأول: لا شيء من الإنسان حجر، وعند عكسه يعود إلى عين المقدمة السالبة. وإن قيل إن هذا ليس بدور، فليس إلا على النحو الذي أشرنا إليه من قبل من أخذ لازم النتيجة في بيان ما كانت النتيجة متوقفة عليه، وهو أراد القائل بقوله أن الدور ليس من شرطه إلا عكس واحد، وهذا ففيه عكسان، وإن كان من الاقتران الثاني فأخذ عكس الموجبة وجعلها كبرى للنتيجة ينتج من الاقتران نفسه عين المقدمة الصغرى، وذلك كما لو قيل: لا شيء من الفرس إنسان، وكل ضاحك إنسان، فلا شيء من الفرس ضاحك، فقلت: لا شيء من الفرس ضاحك، وكل إنسان ضاحك، أنتج: لا شيء من الفرس إنسان، فهو عين المقدمة. وأما استنتاج الموجبة من اقتران النتيجة بعكس السالبة فليس ممكناً؛ إذ لا قياس عن سالبتين. اللهم إلا أن تؤخذ النتيجة معدولة ولازم المقدمة سالبة من غير عكس في الاقتران الأول، وأن يؤخذ عكس النتيجة معدولاً مع لازم المقدمة السالبة من غير عكس في الاقتران الثاني. وأما مثال الأول فكما لو قيل: كل إنسان جوهر، ولا شيء من العرض جوهر، فلا شيء من الإنسان عرض، فإنك إذا أخذت لازم السالبة وهو كل ما هو غير عرض فهو جوهر وجعلته كبرى للنتيجة معدولة فقلت: كل إنسان فهو غير عرض، وكل ما هو غير عرض فهو جوهر، أنتج من الاقتران الأول من الشكل الأول: كل إنسان جوهر، وهو عين المقدمة الصغرى. وأما مثال الثاني فكما لو قيل: لا شيء من الإله حادث، وكل معلول حادث، فلا شيء من الإله معلول، فإنك إذا عكست النتيجة وأخذت العكس معدولاً وجعلته صغرى للازم السالبة أنتج من الاقتران الأول من الشكل الأول عين الموضوع، وذلك بأن نقول: كل معلول فهو ليس بإله، وكل ما هو ليس إلهاً فهو حادث، فكل معلول حادث، وهو عين المقدمة الكبرى الموجبة، وقد نبهنا على ما في تحقيق الدور هاهنا.
The production of the negative (conclusion) is only through the second mood of the first figure, and it requires conversion of the conclusion. That is as if it is said: 'Every human is laughing' and 'Nothing from stone is laughing', so 'Nothing from human is stone'. For if you say: 'Every laughing is human' and 'Nothing from human is stone', it produces from the second mood of the first figure: 'Nothing from human is stone', and when converted it returns to the very same negative premise. And if it is said that this is not circular, it is only in the manner we indicated previously of taking the necessary concomitant of the conclusion in explaining what the conclusion depended on, and the speaker intended by his saying that circularity does not require except one conversion, but here there are two conversions. And if it is from the second mood, taking the conversion of the affirmative and making it the major premise for the conclusion produces from the same mood the very same minor premise, as if it is said: 'Nothing from horse is human' and 'Every laughing is human', so 'Nothing from horse is laughing'. Then you said: 'Nothing from horse is laughing' and 'Every human is laughing' produces: 'Nothing from horse is human', which is the very same premise. As for deriving the affirmative from conjoining the conclusion with the conversion of the negative, it is not possible, since there is no syllogism from two negatives. Unless the conclusion is taken as modified and the necessary concomitant of the premise as negative without conversion in the first mood, and the conversion of the conclusion is taken as modified with the necessary concomitant of the negative premise without conversion in the second mood. As for the example of the first: as if it is said: 'Every human is substance' and 'Nothing from accident is substance', so 'Nothing from human is accident'. For if you take the necessary concomitant of the negative, which is 'Everything that is not accident is substance', and make it the major premise for the conclusion modified, saying: 'Every human is not accident' and 'Everything that is not accident is substance', it produces from the first mood of the first figure: 'Every human is substance', which is the very same minor premise. As for the example of the second: as if it is said: 'Nothing from God is originated' and 'Everything caused is originated', so 'Nothing from God is caused'. For if you convert the conclusion and take the conversion modified and make it the minor premise for the necessary concomitant of the negative, it produces from the first mood of the first figure the very same subject, by saying: 'Everything caused is not God' and 'Everything that is not God is originated', so 'Everything caused is originated', which is the very same affirmative major premise. And we have indicated what is in the verification of circularity here.
Source pages p. 439

Commentary
This passage defines and illustrates circular reasoning (دور) in categorical syllogisms. The author explains that to derive a negative conclusion in the first figure's second mood, one must convert the conclusion. An example is given: from 'Every human is laughing' and 'No stone is laughing', we conclude 'No human is stone'. By converting the conclusion to 'Every laughing is human' and using 'No human is stone', we reproduce the original negative premise. The author then addresses an objection that this involves two conversions, not one. He then shows how to derive the minor premise from the conclusion and the converted major premise in the second mood. Next, he explains that deriving an affirmative conclusion from a negative and a converted negative is impossible without using modified (معدولة) propositions. Two detailed examples follow: one from the first mood of the first figure using modified propositions to derive the minor premise, and another from the second mood to derive the major premise. The passage ends with a note that they have already indicated the verification of circularity.

Key Terms
دور
اقتران
عكس
معدولة
نتيجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the first example of circular reasoning ('Every human is laughing; no stone is laughing'), what is the result of converting the conclusion 'No human is stone'?
A) Every stone is human
B) No stone is human
C) Every laughing is human
D) Some human is stone

Key Takeaway
Circular reasoning in syllogisms uses conversion and/or modified propositions to derive a premise from the conclusion, creating a logical circle that invalidates the argument as a proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the first example from the passage ('Every human is laughing...'), write out step by step how the conclusion and one premise can be used to derive the other premise via conversion. Then explain why this is circular.
